Talaq Under Duress: Hanafi View vs Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling that talaq under duress is valid, with Shafi'i objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on talaq under duress, explain the Hanafi proof using qiyas, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection with the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Talaq under duress is valid because the mukrah intends and chooses between two evils.

This lesson examines one of the well-known points of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the validity of talaq pronounced under duress (mukrah). While the Shafi'i school holds that duress negates free choice and thus invalidates the divorce, the Hanafi school argues that the mukrah still possesses intention and relative choice, making the talaq effective. We will walk through the reasoning, the opposing evidence, and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school rules that talaq pronounced by a person under credible threat of death or loss of limb is valid (waqi). This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
Key term: mukrah (المكره) — a person coerced by a credible threat.
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that duress eliminates ikhtiyar (free choice), which is a condition for legal acts. He also cites the hadith: 'Rufi'a 'an ummati al-khata' wa al-nisyan wa ma istukrihu 'alayh' (the mistake, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into are lifted from my ummah).
Shafi'i also distinguishes from the joker (hazil) who has full freedom.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is rational (aqli) and based on qiyas (analogy) with a voluntary person. The mukrah intended to divorce, had legal capacity, and recognised two evils—choosing the lesser (divorce over death/loss of limb). This shows intention and choice, even if he is not satisfied with the outcome. Like the joker (hazil), his lack of satisfaction does not prevent the talaq from taking effect.
The hadith cited by Shafi'i is interpreted by Hanafis as referring to the lifting of sin in the Hereafter, not worldly legal rulings.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a man is forced to divorce his wife and does so, according to Hanafi the divorce counts (the wife is divorced), whereas according to Shafi'i the divorce does not take effect.
This affects remarriage, iddah, and other related rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق المكره واقع خلافا للشافعي. هو يقول إن الإكراه لا يجامع الاختيار وبه يعتبر التصرف الشرعي بخلاف الهازل لأنه مختار في التكلم بالطلاق. ولنا أنه قصد إيقاع الطلاق في منكوحته في حال أهليته فلا يعرى عن قضيته دفعا لحاجته اعتبارا بالطائع. وذلك لأنه عرف الشرين واختار أهونهما وهذا آية القصد والاختيار إلا أنه غير راض بحكمه وذلك غير مخل به كالهازل.
And talaq of the coerced person is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He says that coercion does not coexist with choice, and by choice a legal act is considered, unlike the joker because he (the joker) is free in uttering talaq. Our proof: he (the coerced) intended to effect talaq upon his wife while he had legal capacity, so it (the intention) will not be devoid of its consequence, to fulfill his need, by analogy with the voluntary person. This is because he recognized the two evils and chose the lesser of them, and this is an indication of intention and choice, except that he is not satisfied with its ruling, and that does not affect (the validity) just like the joker.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that talaq pronounced under duress (mukrah) is valid, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i argument is that duress negates free choice (ikhtiyar), and legal acts require choice; a person under duress is similar to a joker? Actually, the joker (hazil) does have choice, so his talaq is valid. The Shafi'i distinguishes. The Hanafi response is that the mukrah intended to effect talaq (he had the intention) and had legal capacity (he is adult and sane), so his act must have its legal consequence. His need (hajah) to escape the threat is met by the talaq being effective. He recognized two evils (loss of life/limb vs loss of wife) and chose the lesser (talaq), which shows intention and choice. He may not be satisfied with the ruling (he does not want to divorce), but that is irrelevant, just as a joker's lack of satisfaction does not prevent talaq from occurring.

Key Terms
المكره (al-mukrah): A person coerced under threat of death or loss of limb by someone capable of carrying out the threat.
الهازل (al-hazil): A person who jests or jokes, uttering talaq without serious intent.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar): Free choice or volition in performing an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Shafi'i, why is talaq under duress not valid?
A) Because the mukrah does not have legal capacity.
B) Because duress eliminates free choice, which is required for legal acts.
C) Because the hadith lifts all rulings from the mukrah.
D) Because the mukrah did not intend talaq.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates talaq under duress based on the principle that the mukrah intends the divorce and chooses the lesser evil, a ruling that contrasts with the Shafi'i position.

Talaq of an Intoxicated Person: Valid as a Deterrent
Ruling that talaq by an intoxicated person is valid, with dissenting views from Karkhi and Tahawi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that the talaq of an intoxicated person is valid and distinguish the dissenting intra-Hanafi opinion of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi.

Core Idea
Talaq of an intoxicated person is valid because his loss of intellect is due to a sinful act, so the law treats him as sane to deter drinking.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling: a person who becomes severely drunk and then pronounces talaq is held to have issued a valid divorce. The school's reasoning is based on a deterrent principle, and it contrasts with the opinion of two prominent Hanafi scholars, al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists balance textual analogy with the objective of preventing harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as stated in the text: 'Talaq of the intoxicated person is valid' (waqi'). This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah).
The term 'sakran' refers to someone so intoxicated they cannot distinguish earth from sky.
2
Identify the dissenting intra-Hanafi opinion
Al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi, both early Hanafi authorities, held that such talaq does not occur. Also note that one of Imam al-Shafi'i's two opinions agrees with them.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the dissenting view is not from outside the madhhab.
3
Follow the dissenting proof
Their analogy: a valid divorce requires sound intention (qasd), which depends on intellect. Since the intoxicated lacks intellect, the talaq should be invalid, just as talaq is invalid when intellect is lost due to a non-sinful cause like medication (bhenj/dawa).
This is a straightforward qiyas on the basis of a common attribute (loss of intellect).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi position distinguishes based on the cause of the loss of intellect. When the cause is sinful (drinking alcohol), the law considers the intellect legally 'remaining' (hukman) as a punishment (zajran). The example in the text: if drinking causes a headache that then leads to loss of intellect, the talaq is not valid because the intellectual loss is not directly from the sinful act? Actually, the text says: if he drinks and then gets a headache and loses intellect from the headache, his talaq does not occur. This shows the ruling applies only when intoxication itself is the direct cause.
Key principle: a person cannot benefit from his own sin by escaping legal consequences.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that talaq of an intoxicated person is valid (the view of Abu Hanifa, supported by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The authors of Hidayah and Ashraf al-Hidayah prefer this view.
This is also the majority view among later Hanafi jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق السكران واقع واختار الكرخى والطحاوى أنه لا يقع وهو أحد قولي الشافعي لأن صحة القصد بالعقل وهو زائل العقل فصار كزواله بالبنج والدواء. ولنا أنه زال بسبب هو معصية فجعل باقيا حكما زجرا له حتى لو شرب فصدع وزال عقله بالصداع نقول أنه لا يقع طلاقه.
And talaq of the intoxicated person is valid. Al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi chose (the opinion) that it does not occur, and it is one of two sayings of al-Shafi'i. Because correctness of intention is through intellect, and he (the intoxicated) has lost intellect, so it becomes like its loss through henbane and medicine. Our proof: that his intellect ceased due to a cause that is sinful, so it (the intellect) is considered legally remaining as a punishment for him. Even if he drinks and then gets a headache and loses his intellect due to the headache, we say his talaq does not occur.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
The passage states the Hanafi ruling that talaq pronounced by an intoxicated person (sakran) is valid (waqi'). Al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi (Hanafi scholars) preferred the view that it is not valid, and this is also one of two positions of al-Shafi'i. Their argument is that a valid intention requires intellect, and the sakran lacks intellect, similar to someone who loses intellect due to medication (bhenj/dawa). The Hanafi response is that the intoxication is a sinful act (shurb al-khamr is haram), so as a punishment (zajran) the person is treated as if he still had his intellect (hukman), meaning his talaq is effective. The text gives an example: if someone drinks then gets a headache (not from sin) and loses his intellect from that headache, then his talaq would not occur, because the cause of loss of intellect is not sinful.

Key Terms
السكران (al-sakran): A person intoxicated to the extent that he cannot distinguish between earth and sky (i.e., severely drunk).
زجراً (zajran): As a punishment or deterrent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person drinks alcohol and gets intoxicated, then divorces his wife, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, his talaq is:
A) Invalid because he lacks intellect.
B) Valid because the intoxication is from a sinful act.
C) Valid only if he was aware of what he was saying.
D) Invalid unless he confirms it after sobering up.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the talaq of a severely intoxicated person is valid as a deterrent against self-induced loss of intellect through sinful acts.

Validity of a Coerced Divorce (Mukrah)
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i evidence on coerced divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of a coerced divorce and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of lifting.

Core Idea
A coerced divorce is valid in Hanafi fiqh because intention to choose the lesser harm suffices, and the hadith lifts only Hereafter accountability.

This lesson examines the classical Hanafi position on divorce under coercion (ikrah). The Hanafi school holds that such a divorce is valid, even if the person is not pleased with it. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, citing the well-known hadith 'Rufi'a 'an ummati al-khata' wa al-nisyan wa ma istukrihu 'alayhi.' Understanding this dispute requires us to distinguish between worldly legal effects and Hereafter accountability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding a divorce pronounced under coercion.
The ruling is that the divorce is valid (waqi').
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
State the Shafi'i position and the hadith they use as evidence.
Shafi'i argues the hadith lifts all rulings for coerced acts.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi commentator distinguishes worldly and Hereafter accountability regarding the hadith.
Examples: diyah for accidental killing, sajdah sahw for forgetful omission.
4
Follow the Hanafi proof
Examine the Hanafi own positive evidence: the coerced person intends to avoid the greater harm, analogized to a willing person.
Qiyas: mukrah is like a willing person in that both have capacity and choice between harms.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in rulings between the schools.
A Hanafi judge would enforce the divorce; a Shafi'i judge might not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جائے گی۔ اس لئے بازل طلاق کا متکام کرنے میں مختار ہے۔ حضرت امام شافعی حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کے ارشاد رفع عن امتى الخطــاء والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه سے بھی استدلال کرتے ہیں۔ یعنی میری امت سے خطاء اور نسیان اور جس پر مجبور کیا گیا ہے اس کو اٹھالیا گیا۔ لیکن اس حدیث کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حدیث میں آخرت میں رفع حکم مراد ہے نہ کہ دنیا میں ۔ یعنی خطا اور نسیان کی وجہ سے آخرت میں مواخذہ نہیں ہوگا۔ البتہ دنیا میں خطا و نسیان وغیرہ پر احکام مرتب ہوں گے مثلا قتل خطاء میں اگر چہ آخرت میں مواخذہ نہیں ہو گیا لیکن دنیا میں دیت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح نماز میں اگر نسیانا واجب ترک ہو گیا تو گو آخرت میں اس پر مواخذہ نہیں ہوگا لیکن دنیا میں سجدہ سہو واجب ہوتا ہے۔ بہر حال اس حدیث کو استدلال میں پیش کرنا درست نہیں۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مکرہ نے اپنی منکوحہ پر طلاق واقع کرنے کا ارادہ کیا ہے دار انحالیکہ اسکو طلاق واقع کرنے کی لیاقت بھی ہے۔ پس یہ قصد طلاق اپنے مقتضی اور حکم سے خالی نہ جائے گا تاکہ تخلف حكم عن العلت لازم نہ آئے اور تا کہ مکرہ کی حاجت دفع ہو جائے ۔ مگرہ کی حاجت یہ ہے کہ جس چیز سے اس کو ڈرایا گیا ہے اس سے چھٹکارا پا جائے۔ اور اس مکرہ کو قیاس کیا گیا ہے طائع پر ۔ یعنی جس طرح اپنی رضا اور اختیار سے طلاق دینے کی صورت میں طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ اسی طرح مکرہ کے طلاق دینے پر بھی واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور یہ قصد طلاق اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ مکرہ کے سامنے دو برا ئیں تھیں۔ ایک جان کا ضائع ہونا دوسرے بیوی کا ضائع ہونا۔ یعنی اگر طلاق دے تو بیوی گئی اور طلاق نہ دے تو جان گئی۔ اس شخص نے ان دونوں برائیوں میں سے آسان ( وقوع طلاق) کو اختیار کر لیا۔ اور دو برائیوں کو پہچانا اور اہوں کو اختیار کرنا یہی قصد اور اختیار کی علامت ہے۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ مکرہ نے اپنے اختیار سے طلاق دی ہے نہ کہ بلا اختیار ۔ ہاں زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جاسکتا ہے کہ مکرہ ایقاع طلاق کے حکم ( وقوع طلاق) پر راضی نہیں تھا مگر ہم کہتے ہیں کہ ایقاع طلاق ) پر عدم رضا وقوع طلاق میں مخل نہیں ۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ بغیر رضا مندی کے بھی طلاق واقع ہو سکتی ہے۔ جیسے بازل کی طلاق ، وقوع طلاق پر عدم رضا کے باوجود واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔
It will occur. Therefore, the mature (bazl) is free in making divorce effective. Imam Shafi'i also deduces from the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Lifted from my ummah are error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into.' That is, error, forgetfulness, and that which one is forced upon are lifted from my ummah. But the answer to this hadith is that the lifting in the hadith means in the Hereafter, not in this world. That is, because of error and forgetfulness there will be no accountability in the Hereafter. However, in this world rulings are attached to error and forgetfulness, for example in accidental killing, although there is no accountability in the Hereafter, in this world blood money (diyah) is obligatory. Similarly, in prayer if an obligatory act is omitted forgetfully, although there will be no accountability for it in the Hereafter, in this world prostration of forgetfulness (sajdah al-sahw) becomes obligatory. In any case, presenting this hadith as evidence is not correct. Our evidence is that the coerced person intended to effect divorce upon his wife, and he also has the capacity to effect divorce. So this intent of divorce will not be empty of its requirement and ruling, so that the non-occurrence of the ruling due to the cause does not become necessary, and so that the need of the coerced person is fulfilled. The need of the coerced person is to be freed from that which he was threatened. And this coerced person is analogized to the willing person. That is, just as when one gives divorce willingly and by choice, divorce occurs, similarly upon the coerced person giving divorce it will also occur. And this intent of divorce is known from the fact that the coerced person had two evils before him: one the loss of life, the other the loss of the wife. That is, if he gives divorce, the wife is lost, and if he does not give divorce, his life is lost. This person chose the easier of these two evils (occurrence of divorce). And recognizing two evils and choosing the lighter one is a sign of intent and choice. So it is proven that the coerced person gave divorce by his choice, not without choice. Yes, at most it can be said that the coerced person was not pleased with the ruling of effecting divorce (occurrence of divorce), but we say that lack of pleasure in effecting divorce does not impede the occurrence of divorce. The meaning is that divorce can occur without pleasure, just like the divorce of the mature person (bazl), which occurs despite lack of pleasure in its occurrence.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of a divorce pronounced under coercion (mukrah). The Hanafi position is that such a divorce is valid. Imam Shafi'i argues using the hadith 'rafi' al-qalam' that one is not accountable for coerced acts. The Hanafi commentator responds by distinguishing between worldly and Hereafter accountability: the hadith refers to Hereafter exoneration, not worldly legal effects. He then presents the Hanafi proof: the coerced person still intends to divorce, and has the capacity to do so; his intent is not nullified. The analogy is to a willing person who divorces. The person chooses the lesser harm (divorce) over the greater harm (death), indicating choice and intention. Finally, the text points out that lack of pleasure (rida) does not prevent divorce, as seen in the case of a bazl (mature person) who divorces without being pleased. The passage does not specify who 'bazl' refers to, but context suggests a person who divorces under some pressure but not coercion. The key point is that coercion does not negate the validity of divorce in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مکرہ (mukrah): A person who is coerced or forced to do something against his will, specifically in the context of pronouncing divorce.
بازل (bāzil): A mature person who has reached the age of legal responsibility (probably meant as 'bāligh'), used here as someone who divorces without being coerced.
قصد (qasd): Intention or purpose; the coerced person still has a purpose (to avoid harm), which constitutes a valid intent for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the hadith 'Rufi'a...' not invalidate a coerced divorce?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission.
B) The hadith applies only to accountability in the Hereafter, not to worldly legal effects.
C) The hadith does not mention divorce specifically.
D) The Hanafi school does not rely on hadith evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a divorce pronounced under coercion is valid because the coerced person retains the intention to choose the lesser harm, and the hadith of lifting applies only to Hereafter accountability.

Divorce by an Intoxicated Person (Sakran): Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of divorce by an intoxicated person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the divorce of an intoxicated person and explain the rationale based on the sinful cause, identifying the key points of disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The divorce of an intoxicated person is valid because the loss of reason is due to a sin, so accountability is presumed.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi dispute: whether the divorce of a person in extreme intoxication (sakran) is valid. While the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds it valid, two prominent Hanafi imams—Karakhi and Tahawi—disagree, aligning partially with the Shafi'i view. The central point of contention is the role of the cause of intoxication: is it a sinful act that warrants legal deterrence, or does the loss of reason negate intention entirely? We will explore the proofs on both sides and the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position and its proof
Start by identifying what the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule regarding the divorce of an intoxicated person. Then locate their key evidence: the intoxication is caused by a sin (drinking alcohol), so the person is treated as having reason for deterrence.
The proof is explicitly stated: 'zaala bi sabab huwa ma'siyyah fa ju'ila baqiyan hukman zajaran lahu.'
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view and its evidence
Next, consider the position of Imams Karakhi and Tahawi: the divorce is not valid. They argue that intention requires sound reason, and the intoxicated person lacks it, analogous to loss of reason by lawful means (hemp, medicine).
This parallels one of Imam al-Shafi'i's positions.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing qiyas
The majority responds by distinguishing the cause: the loss of reason from intoxication is sinful, while loss from hemp/medicine is not. Therefore, the analogy fails because the effective cause (illa) differs.
The commentator further notes that if the intoxication leads to a headache that removes reason, the divorce is not valid because the cause is no longer sinful.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position and practical consequence
The majority view is the operative one for fatwa. Practically, this means a Hanafi judge will consider the divorce valid, whereas those following Karakhi/Tahawi would not.
This is a clear example of thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of disagreement).

Source Text

طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ اور امام کرخی اور امام طحاوی کا پسندیدہ مذہب یہ ہے کہ طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ یہی امام شافعی کا ایک قول ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ قصد اور ارادہ کا صحیح ہو نا عقل کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے اور یہ شخص زائل العقل ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ عقل کا بھنگ یا دواء سے زائل ہو جانا اور ان دونوں میں بالا تفاق طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی۔ پس اسی طرح نشہ کی حالت میں اگر طلاق دی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ ہماری دلیل یہ کا طلاق کا ارادہ کرنا بھی صحیح ہو گا۔ اور جب ارادہ طلاق صحیح ہے تو طلاق واقع بھی ہوگی۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے شراب پی لی پھر اس کی وجہ سے دردسر ہو گیا اور در دسر کی وجہ سے اس کی عقل زائل ہو گئی تو اس صورت میں ہم بھی کہتے ہیں کہ اس کی دی ہوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں زوال عقل معصیت کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ دردسر کی وجہ سے ہے۔ فوائد مصنف ہدایہ کی عبارت میں تسامح ہے وہ یہ کہ مصنف نے نشہ کی وجہ سے اس کو زائل العقل قرار دیا ہے۔ حالانکہ ایسا نہیں کیونکہ وہ شریعت کے احکام کا مخاطب ہے اور خطاب بلا عقل نہیں ہوتا۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ وہ مغلوب العقل ہے۔ جواب ... چونکہ مغلوب کا معدوم ہوتا ہے اس وجہ سے اس پر زوال کا اطلاق کیا گیا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
And the divorce of an intoxicated person is valid. And the choice of Karakhi and Tahawi is that it is not valid, and it is one of the two sayings of Shafi'i, because the soundness of intention is with reason, and he is one whose reason is gone, so it becomes like its removal by hemp or medicine. And our evidence is that his reason was removed due to a cause that is a sin, so it is treated as remaining valid for deterrence. So much so that if he drank and then got a headache and his reason was removed due to the headache, we say his divorce does not occur. Translation: And the divorce of a person intoxicated with wine is valid. And Imam Karakhi and Imam Tahawi's preferred (madhab) is that it will not occur. And this is one of the two sayings of Imam Shafi'i. This is because the soundness of intention is with reason, and that person is one whose reason is gone. So it becomes like its removal by hemp or medicine. And our evidence is that his reason was removed due to a cause that is a sin, so it is treated as remaining valid for deterrence. So much so that if he drank, then got a headache, and his reason was removed due to the headache, we say his divorce does not occur. Explanation: In the phrase, 'sakran' refers to a person who is in extreme intoxication such that he cannot distinguish between the sky and the earth. The issue is that if a person gives divorce to his wife in extreme intoxication, according to us divorce will occur. And Imam Karakhi and Imam Tahawi's preferred madhab is that divorce will not occur. This is one of the sayings of Imam Shafi'i. The evidence is that the soundness of intention and will is with reason, and this person is one whose reason is gone. So it becomes like the removal of reason by hemp or medicine, and in both of those, divorce does not occur by consensus. So similarly, if he gives divorce while intoxicated, divorce will not occur. Our evidence is that this person's reason was removed due to a cause that is a sin and disobedience, i.e., from drinking wine etc. Therefore, to deter and rebuke this person, his reason is considered remaining in effect. So when his reason is considered remaining, his intention to divorce is also sound. And when the intention to divorce is sound, divorce will occur. For this reason, if a person drank wine, then a headache occurred, and due to the headache his reason was removed, in that case we also say that his divorce is not valid. Because in that case the removal of reason is not due to sin, but due to headache. Benefits: There is a laxity in the author of al-Hidayah's expression: he considered him as one whose reason is removed due to intoxication, but it is not so because he is addressed by the rulings of Shari'ah, and address without reason is not possible. At most it can be said that he is one whose reason is overwhelmed. Answer: Since the overwhelmed one's reason is considered non-existent, therefore the term 'removed' is applied to him. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (with Karakhi and Tahawi) and an inter-madhhab dispute (with Shafi'i) on the validity of divorce by an intoxicated person. The Hanafi majority ruling (from Abu Hanifa and his direct students) is that such a divorce is valid, despite the loss of reason, because the intoxication results from a sinful act (drinking alcohol). As a punishment and deterrent, the person is treated as if he still had reason, so his divorce is effective. Karakhi and Tahawi (both Hanafi imams) hold that it is not valid because reason is essential for intention, and the person lacks reason. The Shafi'i school also has a view that it is not valid (analogizing to loss of reason by lawful means like medicine). The passage clarifies the Hanafi proof: the cause of intoxication is sinful, so the person is held accountable; the divorce is valid. However, if the person drinks lawfully (e.g., headache from illness), then his reason is removed by a non-sinful cause, and his divorce would not be valid. The commentary further notes that the author of al-Hidayah called the intoxicated person 'za'il al-'aql' (one whose reason is gone), but this is imprecise; he is actually 'maghlub al-'aql' (one whose reason is overwhelmed). The answer explains that calling the overwhelmed one 'removed' is a figure of speech.

Key Terms
سکران (sakrān): A person in extreme intoxication, unable to distinguish basic things (like sky and earth).
زائل العقل (zā'il al-'aql): One whose reason is gone; the commentator notes this term is a simplification for the intoxicated person, who is actually overwhelmed (maghlub) not entirely devoid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, why is the divorce of an intoxicated person valid?
A) Because the person could still reason properly.
B) Because the intoxication was caused by a sin, so the person is punished by treating him as having reason.
C) Because Karakhi and Tahawi agreed with the majority.
D) Because the person was not fully intoxicated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the divorce of an intoxicated person is valid due to the sinful cause, treating reason as present for deterrence; this is the mufta bihi position despite the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Validity of a Mute Person's Divorce by Gesture
Ruling that a mute person's divorce by gesture is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mute person's divorce by gesture and explain the proof that customary gesture substitutes for speech.

Core Idea
A mute person's gesture is legally equivalent to speech for all contracts.

How does a person who cannot speak perform a divorce or sale? The Hanafi school holds that their customary gesture is valid for all legal acts, based on the principle that necessity (hajah) allows substitution of gesture for speech. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof from the source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that the divorce of a mute person by gesture is valid (واقع). The ruling is that the divorce takes effect.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is that gesture has become customary (معهودة) and thus is placed in the position of speech (العبارة) to fulfill the need (دفعا للحاجة).
Custom here means it is a habitual and recognized means of communication for the mute.
3
Understand the scope
The commentary clarifies that this applies to all contracts: marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, purchase.
This is a general rule for all legal expressions (nikah, talaq, itq, bay', shira').
4
Note the condition
The Hanafi ruling is unconditional: whether the mute person can write or not, the gesture is valid.
Some Shafi'is distinguish, as will be seen in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق الاخرس واقع بالاشارة لانها صارت معهودة فاقيمت مقام العبارة دفعا للحاجة وسيأتيك وجوهه في آخر الكتاب ان شاء الله ترجمہ اور گونگے کی طلاق اشارے سے واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اشارہ معہود و معین ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اشارہ عبارت کے قائم مقام ہوگا حاجت کو دفع کرنے کیلئے اور عنقریب اس کی وجوہ آخر کتاب میں آجائیں گی انشاء اللہ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ گونگے کی طلاق اشارے سے واقع ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ گونگے کا اشارہ معہود و معروف ہے۔ لہذاوہ دلالت میں عبارت کے مانند ہو گا۔ پس اس کے اشارے سے اس کا نکاح، طلاق، عقاق، بیج اور شراء سب صحیح ہوں گے۔ خواہ وہ گونگا کتابت پر قادر ہو یا کتاب پر قادر نہ ہو۔ اور بعض شوافع کہتے ہیں کہ اگر وہ کتابت پر قادر ہے تو اشارے سے اس کی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ کتابت جو اشارے کے مقابلہ میں اولیٰ ہے اس سے ضرورت پوری ہوگئی۔
And the divorce of a mute person is valid by gesture, because it has become customary, so it is placed in the position of speech to fulfill the need. And its aspects will come to you at the end of the book, if Allah wills. Translation: And the divorce of a mute person by gesture will be valid. Because the gesture is established and definite. For this reason, gesture will stand in place of speech to fulfill the need. And soon its aspects will come at the end of the book, if Allah wills. Explanation: The issue is that the divorce of a mute person by gesture will be valid. The proof is that the gesture of a mute person is habitual and known. Therefore, it is like speech in indication. So by his gesture, his marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, and purchase are all valid, whether he is able to write or not. And some Shafi'is say that if he is able to write, his divorce by gesture is not valid, because writing, which is preferable to gesture, fulfills the need.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof for the validity of divorce by a mute person through gesture. The text first gives the ruling: the divorce of a mute person is valid by gesture. The proof is that the gesture of a mute person is customary (ma'hud) and known, so it stands in place of spoken words (ibarah) to fulfill the need (hajah). The author promises to elaborate later in the book. The commentary expands: the gesture of a mute person is equivalent to speech in conveying meaning; thus all his contracts (marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, purchase) are valid. It notes a Shafi'i ikhtilaf: if the mute person can write, his gesture does not suffice because writing is superior. The Hanafi view does not distinguish: both gesture and writing are acceptable if he is mute, because the need is to communicate, and gesture is already customary.

Key Terms
اخرس (akhras): A mute person who cannot speak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Yes, it is valid even if he can write.
C) Only if he cannot write.
D) Only if witnesses are present.

Key Takeaway
A mute person's gesture is legally equivalent to spoken words for all contracts in Hanafi law.

Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on Gesture vs. Writing for the Mute
Comparison of Hanafi and Shafi'i views on the effect of writing ability on a mute person's gesture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on a mute person's gesture when he can write, and identify the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
The dispute centers on whether writing is a stricter requirement than gesture for a mute person's legal acts.

While the Hanafi school universally accepts a mute person's gesture, some Shafi'is require writing if possible. This lesson examines the opposing dalil and the Hanafi response, clarifying why the Hanafi ruling is more comprehensive and widely followed.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school rules that a mute person's gesture is valid for all legal acts regardless of his ability to write.
This is based on the custom of gesture being equivalent to speech.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some Shafi'is say that if the mute person can write, his gesture is not valid because writing is a superior means of expression.
They argue that writing fulfills the need more clearly.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Writing is 'awla' (preferable) to gesture, so when writing is possible, the need is met by writing and gesture becomes unnecessary.
This is a qiyas based on the principle of using the most effective means.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis do not require writing because gesture is already customary and directly substitutes speech. Both are effective; there is no requirement to use the 'best' method.
The principle of 'دفع الحاجة' (fulfilling the need) is satisfied by gesture.
5
Determine the thamarah
Practically, if a mute person uses gesture to divorce while being able to write, the Hanafi school validates the divorce, while the Shafi'i school would require writing for validity. This affects the actual application of talaq and other contracts.
Also applies to marriage, sale, etc.

Number of Divorces: Slave vs Free Woman
Dispute with Shafi'i on number of divorces for slave and free women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the number of divorces for slave and free women, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The number of divorces depends on the wife's status, not the husband's.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on a fundamental issue in family law: who determines the maximum number of divorces? The Hanafi school holds that it is the wife's status (free or slave), while the Shafi'i school ties it to the husband. We will analyze the proofs from both sides and see the practical impact of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: a slave woman can be divorced twice, a free woman three times, regardless of the husband's status.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i argues the number depends on the husband: a free man gives three divorces, a slave man gives two.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Analyze the hadith 'Divorce is by men, waiting period is by women' and the reasoning that ownership (malikiyyah) is an honor complete in a free man.
The Shafi'i also use qiyas on ownership.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof
Study the specific hadith 'Divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstruations' and the principle that slavery halves blessings.
This hadith directly addresses the count based on the woman.
5
Determine the practical difference
Apply the differences to specific marriages: e.g., free man + slave woman → Hanafi: 2 divorces; Shafi'i: 3 divorces.
This is called thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق الامة ثنتان حراكان زوجها أو عبدا وطلاق الحرة ثلاث حراكان زوجها أو عبداً وقال الشافعي عدد الطلاق معتبر بحال الرجال لقوله عليه السلام الطلاق بالرجال والعدة بالنساء ولان صفة المالكية كرامة والأدمية مستدعيه لها ومعنى الأدمية في الحر اكمل فكانت مالكيته ابلغ واكثر ولنا قوله عليه السلام طلاق الامة ثنتان وعدتها حيضتان ولان حل المحلية نعمة فى حقها وللرق اثرى فى تنصيف النعم الا
And the divorce of a slave woman is two, whether her husband is free or a slave. And the divorce of a free woman is three, whether her husband is free or a slave. And al-Shafi'i said: The number of divorces is considered by the condition of the men, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Divorce is by men, and waiting period is by women.' And because the quality of ownership (malikiyyah) is an honor, and humanity (adamiyyah) requires it, and the meaning of humanity is more complete in the free man, so his ownership is more effective and greater. And our proof is his saying (peace be upon him): 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstruations.' And because the resolution of marital bond (hill al-mahalliyyah) is a blessing upon her, and slavery has an effect in dividing blessings, except...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the number of divorces a husband can give to a slave woman versus a free woman. The Hanafi position is that the number of divorces is determined by the status of the wife, not the husband. A slave woman can only be divorced twice (whether she is married to a freedman or a slave), and a free woman can be divorced three times. Shafi'i argues that it depends on the husband: if the husband is free, he can give three divorces; if a slave, only two. This is based on the hadith 'Divorce is by men, waiting period is by women,' and reasoning that ownership (malikiyyah) is an honor that is complete in a free man. Hanafi proofs include a specific hadith stating that the divorce of a slave woman is two, and the principle that slavery halves blessings (so a slave woman's rights are reduced). The passage ends with 'illa' (except), suggesting a continuation.

Key Terms
امة (amah): A female slave, whose marriage and divorce rights are halved compared to a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Divorce by a Mute Person via Gesture
Inter-madhhab dispute on gesture divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mute person's divorce by gesture, identify the Shafi'i objection, and explain why gesture suffices despite ability to write.

Core Idea
A mute person's gesture is valid for divorce even if he can write.

Can a person who cannot speak express a valid divorce through hand signs or nods? The Hanafi school affirms this, but some Shafi'is disagree when writing is possible. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the act (divorce by a mute person) and the ruling (valid). Note the key term 'ma'hūd' (conventionally understood).
The passage states: 'طلاق الاخرس واقع بالاشارة'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Some Shafi'is hold that if the mute person can write, gesture does not suffice. Understand their logic: writing is superior.
Opposing school not named explicitly but referred to as 'بعض شوافع'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues that writing fulfills the need better than gesture, so gesture becomes unnecessary.
The text does not quote the opponent's full proof; we infer from the mention of 'کتابت اولیٰ'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that gesture is also conventional and sufficient, even if writing is possible. The need (hājah) is repelled by gesture.
The author promises more detail later: 'وسيأتيك وجوهه في آخر الكتاب'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In Hanafi practice, such divorce is valid; in Shafi'i practice, it would be invalid if the mute person can write.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

گونگے کی اشارہ سے طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے وطلاق الاخرس واقع بالاشارة لانها صارت معهودة فاقيمت مقام العبارة دفعا للحاجة وسيأتيك وجوهه في آخر الكتاب ان شاء الله ترجمہ اور گونگے کی طلاق اشارے سے واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اشارہ معہود و معین ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اشارہ عبارت کے قائم مقام ہوگا حاجت کو دفع کرنے کیلئے اور عنقریب اس کی وجوہ آخر کتاب میں آجائیں گی انشاء اللہ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ گونگے کی طلاق اشارے سے واقع ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ گونگے کا اشارہ معہود و معروف ہے۔ لہذاوہ دلالت میں عبارت کے مانند ہو گا۔ پس اس کے اشارے سے اس کا نکاح، طلاق، عقاق، بیج اور شراء سب صحیح ہوں گے۔ خواہ وہ گونگا کتابت پر قادر ہو یا کتاب پر قادر نہ ہو۔ اور بعض شوافع کہتے ہیں کہ اگر وہ کتابت پر قادر ہے تو اشارے سے اس کی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ کتابت جو اشارے کے مقابلہ میں اولیٰ ہے اس سے ضرورت پوری ہوگئی۔
Divorce by a mute person occurs through gesture. And the divorce of a mute person is valid by gesture, because it has become well-known, so it is placed in the position of speech to repulse need. And its aspects will come to you at the end of the book, God willing. Translation: And the divorce of a mute person will be valid by gesture. Because the gesture is well-known and specific. For this reason, the gesture will stand in place of speech to repulse need, and soon its aspects will come at the end of the book, God willing. Explanation: The issue is that a mute person's divorce occurs by gesture. The proof is that the mute person's gesture is well-known and recognized. Therefore, in indication it is like speech. So by his gesture, his marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, and purchase are all valid, whether the mute person is able to write or not. And some Shafi'is say that if he is able to write, then his divorce by gesture does not take effect, because writing, which is superior to gesture, fulfills the need.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a mute person (al-akhras) can validly pronounce divorce through gestures (ishārah). The Hanafi school holds that because the mute person's gesture is conventionally understood (ma'hūd) within his community, it takes the place of explicit speech (ibārah) to fulfill the need for communication. Consequently, all legal acts—marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, purchase—are valid through gesture. Some Shafi'i jurists object: if the mute person is literate, he should use writing, which is superior to gesture, and only if he cannot write is gesture allowed. The Hanafi response, implied here, is that gesture is also a valid means even if writing is possible, since gesture is habitual and sufficient. The author notes that further details will come later in the book.

Key Terms
الاخرس (al-akhras): A mute person who cannot speak.
الاشارة (al-ishārah): Gesture, a nonverbal sign conventionally understood.
معہود (ma'hūd): Well-known or conventionally agreed upon; the gesture is widely understood among people.
کتابت (kitābah): Writing; the ability to write one's intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a mute person who can write divorces his wife by gesture, is the divorce valid?
A) Yes, because his gesture is well-known and substitutes for speech.
B) No, because writing is superior and should be used.
C) Yes, only if he does not know how to write.
D) No, because gesture is never valid for divorce.

Key Takeaway
A mute person's divorce by gesture is valid in Hanafi fiqh, regardless of ability to write, because the gesture is a conventional substitute for speech.

Divorce by an Intoxicated Person: Hanafi Position and Rationale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of divorce by an intoxicated person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by an intoxicated person, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the ruling to scenarios based on the cause of intoxication.

Core Idea
A divorce from sin-driven intoxication is valid in Hanafi law because the sinner is held accountable.

In this lesson, we examine the nuanced ruling on divorce by an intoxicated person. The Hanafi school holds that such a divorce takes effect if the intoxication results from a sinful act, as a deterrent. However, some Hanafi jurists like Imam al-Karkhi and Imam al-Tahawi disagree, aligning with one view of Imam al-Shafi'i. We will unpack the proofs and understand the exception to this rule.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that divorce by an intoxicated person is valid (sahih) according to the Hanafi school, with the condition that the intoxication is caused by a sinful act.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that Imam al-Karkhi, Imam al-Tahawi, and one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i hold that divorce does not take effect, because the person lacks sound intellect.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent argues by analogy: just as divorce by a person whose intellect is lost due to cannabis or medicine is invalid by consensus, so too divorce by an intoxicated person should be invalid.
The opponent treats all causes of lost intellect equally.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the cause: when the loss of intellect is due to sin (like drinking wine), the person is legally considered sane as a punishment, making his divorce valid.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the principle of deterrence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge would rule the divorce effective, while the opposing view would leave the marriage intact. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by the majority of Hanafi scholars.
Note the exception: if the loss of intellect is due to a non-sinful intermediary (e.g., headache from drinking), divorce does not take effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چہارم کتاب الطلاق کہ اگر کوئی شخص انتہائی نشہ کی حالت میں اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دیدے تو ہمارے نزدیک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ اور امام کرخی اور امام طحاوی کا پسندیدہ مذہب یہ ہے کہ طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ یہی امام شافعی کا ایک قول ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ قصد اور ارادہ کا صحیح ہو نا عقل کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے اور یہ شخص زائل العقل ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ عقل کا بھنگ یا دواء سے زائل ہو جانا اور ان دونوں میں بالا تفاق طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی۔ پس اسی طرح نشہ کی حالت میں اگر طلاق دی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص کی عقل ایسے سبب سے زائل ہوئی ہے جو معصیت اور گناہ ہے یعنی شرب خمر وغیرہ سے لہذا اس شخص کو زجر و توبیخ کرنے کیلئے اس کی عقل کو حکما باقی قرار دیا گیا ہے۔ پس جب حکما اس کی عقل باقی ہے تو اس کا طلاق کا ارادہ کرنا بھی صحیح ہو گا۔ اور جب ارادہ طلاق صحیح ہے تو طلاق واقع بھی ہوگی۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے شراب پی لی پھر اس کی وجہ سے دردسر ہو گیا اور در دسر کی وجہ سے اس کی عقل زائل ہو گئی تو اس صورت میں ہم بھی کہتے ہیں کہ اس کی دی ہوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں زوال عقل معصیت کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ دردسر کی وجہ سے ہے۔ فوائد مصنف ہدایہ کی عبارت میں تسامح ہے وہ یہ کہ مصنف نے نشہ کی وجہ سے اس کو زائل العقل قرار دیا ہے۔ حالانکہ ایسا نہیں کیونکہ وہ شریعت کے احکام کا مخاطب ہے اور خطاب بلا عقل نہیں ہوتا۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ وہ مغلوب العقل ہے۔ جواب ... چونکہ مغلوب کا معدوم ہوتا ہے اس وجہ سے اس پر زوال کا اطلاق کیا گیا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume four, Book of Divorce: If a person divorces his wife in a state of extreme intoxication, then according to us divorce takes effect. And Imam al-Karkhi and Imam al-Tahawi's preferred view is that divorce does not take effect. This is also one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i. Their proof is that sound intention and will occur with intellect, and this person is one whose intellect is gone. So it becomes like loss of intellect due to cannabis or medicine, and in both of those, divorce does not take effect by consensus. So similarly, if he divorces in a state of intoxication, divorce will not take effect. Our proof is that this person's intellect has been removed due to a cause that is disobedience and sin, i.e., drinking wine etc. Therefore, in order to rebuke and reprimand this person, his intellect is legally considered remaining. So when his intellect is legally remaining, his intention to divorce is also valid. And when the intention to divorce is valid, divorce will also take effect. This is the reason that if a person drank wine, then due to that he got a headache, and due to the headache his intellect was removed, then in that case we also say that his divorce does not take effect. Because in this case, loss of intellect is not due to sin but due to headache. Benefit: There is imprecision in the wording of the author of al-Hidayah: he called him 'one whose intellect is gone' due to intoxication, while that is not the case because he is addressed by the rulings of Shari'ah, and address without intellect is not possible. At most it can be said that he is 'one whose intellect is overcome'. Response: Since 'overcome' is [treated as] non-existent, therefore 'gone' was applied to it. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of divorce pronounced by an intoxicated person. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute as well as a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author presents the Hanafi position (attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and his students) that divorce takes effect, and then the opposing view of Imam al-Karkhi and Imam al-Tahawi (who are Hanafi jurists) and one opinion of al-Shafi'i that it does not. The opposing proof is based on the lack of sound intention due to loss of intellect. The Hanafi proof is that the loss of intellect is caused by a sinful act (drinking alcohol), so to punish the sinner, his intellect is legally considered intact, making his intention valid. An exception is given: if the intoxication results indirectly from sin (e.g., drinking leads to headache which causes loss of intellect), then divorce does not take effect because the immediate cause is not sinful. The author then notes a terminological imprecision in the Hidayah: the intoxicated person is called 'zā'il al-'aql' (deprived of intellect) but should be called 'maghlūb al-'aql' (overcome in intellect) since he is still addressed by law. The response reconciles the terms by stating that 'overcome' is treated as 'non-existent' in effect.

Key Terms
زائل العقل (zā'il al-'aql): One whose intellect is completely gone, according to the author of al-Hidayah's description of an intoxicated person.
مغلوب العقل (maghlūb al-'aql): One whose intellect is overcome but not entirely removed; he remains addressed by Shari'ah.
معصیت (ma'ṣiyah): Sin or disobedience; the cause of intoxication (drinking wine) is a sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man drinks wine, gets a severe headache from it, then loses his intellect and pronounces divorce, does the divorce take effect?
A) Yes, because the intoxication is sinful.
B) No, because the loss of intellect is due to the headache, not directly due to sin.
C) Yes, because he intended to drink and thus is accountable.
D) No, because all intoxicated persons' divorces are invalid.

Key Takeaway
A divorce by an intoxicated person is valid in Hanafi law only if the loss of intellect is directly caused by a sinful act; otherwise it is invalid.

Who Determines the Number of Divorces: Husband or Wife?
Inter-madhhab dispute on divorce counts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the number of divorces for slave and free women, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Hanafi: divorce count follows wife's status; Shafi'i: follows husband's status.

This passage addresses one of the classic points of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: when a husband divorces his wife, does the maximum number of permissible divorces depend on whether the wife is free or slave, or on whether the husband is free or slave? The disagreement has real-world consequences, especially in mixed-status marriages. Let's walk through the proofs and understand why each school takes its position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi rule: a slave woman can only be divorced twice, regardless of her husband's status; a free woman three times.
The key phrase: 'طلاق الامة ثنتان' (divorce of a slave woman is two).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State that Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik and Ahmad) hold that the number depends on the husband: a free man can pronounce three divorces, a slave man only two, irrespective of the wife's status.
Opposing proof: 'الطلاق بالرجال والعدة بالنساء'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the Shafi'i textual proof (the hadith) and their rational argument about ownership being a mark of honor connected to humanity.
Their rational proof ties the number to the husband's status because ownership (malakiyyah) is an honor that is more complete in a free man.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the Shafi'i hadith refers to the authority to initiate divorce, not the count. The explicit hadith about the slave woman ('طلاق الامة ثنتان') settles the matter based on her status.
Also the rational argument: marriage is a blessing for the woman, and slavery halves blessings, but the divorce cannot be halved, so two complete divorces are allowed.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the two practical cases where the rulings differ: (a) free woman married to a slave husband: Hanafi allows 3 divorces, Shafi'i only 2; (b) slave woman married to a free man: Hanafi allows only 2, Shafi'i allows 3.
This is the practical consequence that affects actual divorce situations.

ہوگا بیوی آزاد ہو یا باندی۔ دلیل عقلیت یہ ہے کہ مالک ہونے کی صفت کرامت ہے اور ہر وہ چیز جو کرامت ہو آدمیت اس کا تقاضا کرتی ہے۔ کیونکہ آدمی اللہ تعالیٰ کے مکرم کر دینے سے مکرم ہے۔ فرمایا و لقد کرمنا کا مالک ہونا بھی مملوک کے مقابلہ میں زیادہ ابلغ ہو گا اس وجہ سے امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ آزاد مرد تین طلاق کا مالک ہے اور غلام دوکا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہیکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا طلاق الامة ثنتان وعدتها حيضتان حدیث میں لامہ کا الف لام جنس کیلئے ہے پس معنی
And the divorce of a slave woman is two, whether her husband is free or slave, and the divorce of a free woman is three, whether her husband is free or slave. And al-Shafi'i said: The number of divorces is considered by the condition of men, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Divorce is with men, and the waiting period is with women.' And because the attribute of ownership is honor, and humanity requires it, and the meaning of humanity is more complete in a free man, so his ownership is more effective and greater. And our proof is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' And because the permissibility of the marital bond is a blessing for her, and slavery has an effect in halving blessings, except that the knot is not divisible, so two complete knots. And the interpretation of what is narrated that 'pronouncement is by men'. Translation: And the divorces of a slave woman are two, her husband being free or slave, and the divorces of a free woman are three, her husband being free or slave. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: The number of divorces is considered by the condition of men, because of the Prophet's saying: 'Divorce is with men, and the waiting period is with women.' And because the quality of ownership is honor, and humanity necessitates it, and the meaning of humanity is more complete in a free man, so his ownership is more effective and greater. And our proof is the Prophet's saying: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' And because the permissibility of what is lawful (the marriage bond) is a blessing for her, and slavery has an effect in halving blessings, except that the marriage knot is not divisible, so two complete knots. And the interpretation of what is narrated that 'pronouncement is by men'. Explanation: There is a difference of opinion among the Imams regarding the number of divorces. So the Hanafi school is that in the number of divorces, consideration is given to the women. That is, a slave woman has two divorces whether her husband is free or slave; and a free woman has three divorces whether her husband is free or slave. The gist is that a slave woman becomes separated with two divorces and a free woman with three. Imam al-Shafi'i says: In the number of divorces, the condition of men is considered. That is, if a man is free, he is authorized to give three divorces to his wife whether she is free or slave; and if a man is a slave, he is authorized to give two divorces to his wife whether she is free or slave. This is also the school of Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad. The practical consequence of the difference appears in two cases: first, when a free woman is in the marriage of a slave; second, when a slave woman is in the marriage of a free man. So in the first case, according to Hanafis, three divorces can be given; and in the second case, two divorces can be given. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the reverse is true: in the first case, two divorces; in the second case, three. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is the Prophet's statement: 'Divorce is with men, and the waiting period is with women.' The reasoning is that in the hadith, divorce and waiting period are contrasted. And in the waiting period, by consensus, consideration is given to women in terms of number. So if she is a slave, her waiting period is two menstrual cycles (as Hanafis say) or two periods of purity (as Shafi'is say); if she is free, her waiting period is three menstrual cycles or three periods of purity. Therefore, to establish the contrast, in divorce, consideration is given to men: a free man owns three divorces, his wife being free or slave; and a slave man owns two divorces, his wife being free or slave. Their rational proof is that the attribute of ownership is honor, and everything that is honor is necessitated by humanity. Because man is honored by Allah's honoring, as He said: 'Indeed We have honored the children of Adam.' And the meaning of humanity is more complete in a free man, because a free man has capacity for things that a slave does not, e.g., judgeship, guardianship, testimony. So when humanity is more complete in a free man, his ownership is also more complete compared to a slave. For this reason, Imam al-Shafi'i said: A free man owns three divorces and a slave owns two. Our proof is that the Prophet said: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' In the hadith, the 'alif lam' in 'al-ammah' is for the genus, so the meaning...
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute regarding the number of divorces (talaq) a husband can pronounce. The Hanafi position is that the number depends on the status of the wife: a slave woman (amah) can only be divorced twice, regardless of whether her husband is free or slave; a free woman (hurrah) can be divorced three times. Imam al-Shafi'i (and also Malik and Ahmad) hold that the number depends on the status of the husband: a free man can pronounce three divorces irrespective of his wife's status, while a male slave can only pronounce two. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf) appears in two cases: (1) a free woman married to a slave husband—Hanafis allow three divorces, Shafi'is only two; (2) a slave woman married to a free man—Hanafis allow only two divorces, Shafi'is allow three. The Shafi'i proof is the hadith 'al-ṭalāqu bi al-rijāl wa al-'iddatu bi al-nisā'' and a rational argument that ownership is honor, which is more complete in free men. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'ṭalāqu al-amati thintān wa 'iddatuhā ḥayḍatān', indicating that the number is tied to the woman. They also argue that the marriage bond is a blessing for the woman, and slavery reduces blessings; however, since the divorce cannot be divided, the woman's status sets the number. The passage ends with a beginning of interpreting the Shafi'i hadith as referring to the authority to pronounce, not the count.

Key Terms
الامة (al-amah): A female slave; a woman who is owned.
الحرة (al-ḥurrah): A free woman; not a slave.
عدد الطلاق (adad al-ṭalāq): The maximum number of divorces a husband may pronounce before the wife becomes irrevocably separated.

A) Three, because he is free.
B) Two, because she is a slave.
C) Three, because he is the master.
D) Two, because she is under his ownership.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school ties the maximum number of divorces to the wife's status (free or slave), not the husband's, based on the explicit hadith 'طلاق الامة ثنتان'.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Number of Divorces: Wife's Status vs Husband's Status
Dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on the basis for number of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces for a free woman and a slave woman, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on hadith and rational proof.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the number of divorces depends on the wife, not the husband — a slave woman has two, a free woman three.

This lesson explores a classic dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school (also followed by Malik and Ahmad) regarding the basis for determining the maximum number of divorces a husband can give. The Hanafis hold that the wife's status (free or slave) determines the number, while the Shafi'is hold that the husband's status is what matters. We will examine both sides' proofs, the Hanafi response, and the practical consequences that arise when a free woman marries a slave or vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Position
State clearly what the Hanafi school rules: a slave woman (her husband free or slave) can receive a maximum of two divorces, a free woman three. Note that this is irrespective of the husband’s status.
2
Identify the Opposing View
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik and Ahmad) hold that the husband's status determines the number: a free man can give three, a slave two, regardless of the wife's status.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Proof
Shafi'i relies on the hadith 'Al-talaq bi al-rijal wa al-iddah bi al-nisa' and a rational argument linking ownership of divorce to the perfection of humanity in a free man.
4
Examine the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafis rely on the hadith 'Talaq al-amah thintan wa iddatuha haydatan' and a rational argument: marriage is a blessing halved by slavery, but divorce is indivisible, so the number becomes two (not one and a half).
5
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response to the Shafi'i hadith: (1) the correct meaning is that pronouncing divorce is by men, not the number; (2) the hadith is mawquf (stopped at a Companion), not marfu', so it is not a valid proof by Shafi'i's own methodology.

The knot (of marriage) is indivisible, so two complete knots (divorces) are made complete, and the interpretation of what is narrated that the pronouncement is by men. Translation: And a slave woman's divorces are two, her husband be free or slave, and a free woman's divorces are three, her husband be free or slave. And Imam Shafi'i said: The number of divorce depends on the condition of men. Because the Prophet's statement: 'Divorce is with men and the waiting period is with women.' And because the attribute of ownership is an honor, and humanity necessitates it. And the meaning of humanity is more perfect in a free man, so free man's ownership will be greater. And our proof is the Prophet's statement: 'A slave woman's divorce is two and her waiting period is two menstruations.' And because becoming lawful through marriage is a blessing for the woman, and slavery has a role in halving blessings, except that divorce is not divisible, so two complete divorces are made. And the interpretation of that hadith is that pronouncing divorce is in the hands of men. Elaboration: The scholars differ regarding the number of divorces. The Hanafi school holds that the number takes into account the woman: a slave woman has two divorces, a free woman three, regardless of the husband's status. Imam Shafi'i holds that the number takes into account the husband's status: a free man can give three, a slave two, regardless of the wife's status. This is also the view of Malik and Ahmad. The practical difference appears in two cases: (1) a free woman married to a slave: Hanafis allow three divorces, Shafi'is allow two. (2) a slave woman married to a free man: Hanafis allow two, Shafi'is allow three. Shafi'i's proof: the hadith 'Al-talaq bi al-rijal wa al-iddah bi al-nisa'. The reasoning: the hadith contrasts divorce and waiting period; waiting period is agreed to take into account the woman's status, so by analogy divorce must take into account the man's status. Rational proof: ownership is an honor, and honor is necessitated by humanity, which is more perfect in free men. Hanafi's proof: the hadith 'Talaq al-amah thintan wa iddatuha haydatan' (the letter lam is for jins, meaning the species). This shows that the number is based on the woman. Rational proof: marriage is a blessing for the woman, slavery halves blessings, but since divorce is indivisible, it becomes two (not one and a half). The author of Hidayah responds to Shafi'i's hadith: its meaning is 'Iqa' al-talaq bi al-rijal' (pronouncement is by men), not the number. Also, the hadith is mawquf (stopped at a Companion), not marfu', so it is not a valid proof according to Shafi'i's own principles.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the basis for the number of divorces. The Hanafi position is that the number depends on the wife: a slave woman (regardless of husband's status) can be divorced a maximum of two times, a free woman three times. Imam Shafi'i (and Malik and Ahmad) hold that it depends on the husband: a free man can give three divorces, a slave two, regardless of the wife's status. The passage presents both sides' proofs. Shafi'i uses the hadith 'Al-talaq bi al-rijal wa al-iddah bi al-nisa' and a rational argument about honor. The Hanafis use the hadith 'Talaq al-amah thintan' and a rational argument based on the indivisibility of divorce. The Hanafi response to the Shafi'i hadith is twofold: (1) the correct interpretation is that the pronounciation of divorce is by men, not the number; (2) the hadith is mawquf (not marfu'), so it is not authoritative according to Shafi'i's own methodology. This dispute has practical consequences in two cases: a free woman married to a slave and a slave woman married to a free man.

Key Terms
الطلاق بالرجال (al-talaq bi al-rijal): A hadith phrase used by Shafi'is to argue that the number of divorces is tied to the husband's status; Hanafis interpret it as meaning the pronouncement of divorce is by the husband.
طلاق الامة ثنتان (talaq al-amah thintan): The hadith stating that a slave woman's divorces are two, used by Hanafis as primary evidence.
موقوف (mawquf): A hadith whose chain of narration stops at a Companion, not reaching the Prophet. Hanafis argue that the Shafi'i proof hadith is mawquf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Three divorces
C) One divorce
D) It depends on the master's permission

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the number of divorces is determined by the wife's status (free woman = three, slave woman = two), not the husband's status, based on the hadith 'Talaq al-amah thintan' and the rational argument of indivisibility.

The Validity of a Slave's Divorce: Ownership of Marriage
Ruling on slave's divorce with master's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave's divorce is valid when he marries with his master's permission, and identify the master's lack of authority over the divorce.

Core Idea
A slave who marries with permission owns the marriage contract, so only he can divorce.

This lesson presents a clear ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding the divorce of a slave. The key principle is that the ownership of marriage (milku al-nikah) belongs to the slave himself when he marries with his master's permission. Consequently, the master has no right to divorce the slave's wife. This ruling is based on the slave's capacity to own certain rights, which is a distinctive feature of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and permissions
Recognize that the slave marries with explicit permission from his master. This permission grants the slave full authority over the marriage contract.
Permission is essential; marriage without permission may have different rulings.
2
State the ruling for the slave's divorce
The divorce pronounced by the slave is valid and takes immediate effect.
3
State the ruling for the master's divorce
Any divorce pronounced by the master on the slave's wife is null and void.
The master has no authority over the marriage contract.
4
Explain the legal cause (sabab)
The ownership of marriage (milku al-nikah) is a right that belongs to the slave. Since the right to annul the marriage follows ownership, only the slave can exercise it.
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh: the one who owns the contract owns its termination.

The ruling is straightforward and based on the slave's right to own the benefits of the marriage.

Definition of Clear (Sarih) Talaq
Definition of sarih talaq and its legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the words of sarih talaq and state the ruling that no intention is required for them to take effect.

Core Idea
Sarih talaq words are exclusive to divorce — they effect a revocable divorce without needing intention.

In this lesson, we explore the first category of divorce according to pronouncement: sarih (clear) talaq. The Hanafi school distinguishes between clear and metaphorical expressions, and the ruling for clear words is straightforward: they immediately effect a revocable divorce, regardless of the husband's intention. This principle is foundational for understanding how divorce is initiated and why certain statements cannot be undone by claiming a different intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification of divorce
Divorce by pronouncement is divided into two types: sarih (clear) and kinayah (metaphorical). Sarih words are those used exclusively for divorce.
2
Memorize the sarih phrases
The three explicit phrases are: 'anti taliq' (you are divorced), 'muttallaqa' (divorced woman), and 'tallaqtuki' (I divorce you). Any equivalent in other languages also qualifies.
3
Understand the default legal effect
When a sarih word is uttered, a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) takes effect. The husband has the right to take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract, based on the Quranic text (ayat al-raj'ah).
4
Note the absence of intention requirement
Because these words are exclusively used for divorce and their meaning is clear by prevalent usage, no specific intention (niyyah) is needed. The divorce occurs even if the husband was joking, angry, or meant something else.
This is a key Hanafi distinction; other schools may require intention in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: Pronouncement of Divorce. Translation: This chapter is about the pronouncement of divorce. Commentary: Since in the previous chapter the author discussed the essence of divorce and its description as sunni and bid'i, therefore in this chapter he will present the types and kinds of divorce in terms of pronouncement. Divorce is of two types: clear (sarih) and metaphorical (kinayah). So the clear (sarih) is his saying 'You are divorced' and 'divorced woman' and 'I divorce you' – then by this a revocable divorce occurs, because these words are used in divorce and not used in other than it, so it is clear. And it entails the right of return (raj'ah) by text (of the Quran), and it does not need intention, because it is clear in it due to the prevalence of usage. And likewise if he intends separation (ban), because he intended to execute what the law has tied to the end of the 'iddah, then it returns (?)

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on types of talaq by pronouncement. The author divides talaq into sarih (clear) and kinayah (metaphorical). He then defines sarih as using explicit words like 'anti taliq' (you are divorced), 'muttallaqa' (divorced woman), or 'tallaqtuki' (I divorce you). These words are exclusively used for divorce, so they are clear. The effect is that a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs, meaning the husband can take his wife back during the 'iddah without a new contract. This is based on the Quranic text (ayat al-raj'ah). Because these words are so commonly used for divorce, no specific intention (niyyah) is needed; the divorce takes effect regardless of what the husband intended. However, if he intended a final separation (ba'in), the text suggests that it still returns to raj'ah? The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear: sarih words effect a raj'i divorce by default, and if the husband intends ba'in, it is still considered raj'i because he intended to execute what the law has tied to the end of the 'iddah, so it returns (i.e., the divorce is still raj'i). That is the Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Explicit words that are exclusively used for divorce, such as 'anti taliq'.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce: the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
