Slave Woman's Divorce Count: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute about hadith 'al-talaq bi al-rijal'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the number of divorces for a slave woman, identify the Shafi'i evidence, and evaluate the Hanafi response including the reinterpretation of 'al-talaq bi al-rijal' and the hadith's chain criticism.

Core Idea
The number of divorces a slave woman receives depends on her status, not her husband's.

This passage presents a key inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the number of divorces that can be given to a slave woman. The Hanafi school holds that a slave woman can only receive two divorces, based on a rational analogy linking slavery to halving of blessings. The Shafi'i school argues from a hadith that divorce is in the hands of men, implying the husband's status determines the count. The author of Al-Hidayah provides a detailed rebuttal, reinterpreting the hadith and questioning its authenticity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its rational proof
Identify the Hanafi ruling: a slave woman receives only two divorces. Understand the qiyas: slavery halves blessings, so a slave woman's divorce count should be half of a free woman's three, rounded up to two due to indivisibility.
The rational proof is based on the general principle that slavery affects legal capacities.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing evidence
The Shafi'i school relies on the hadith 'al-talaq bi al-rijal', which they interpret to mean that the entire matter of divorce (including the count) is in the hands of men, thus a free husband can give three divorces to his slave wife.
This hadith is the core of the Shafi'i argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: reinterpretation of the hadith's meaning
The Hanafi school reinterprets 'al-talaq bi al-rijal' to mean 'iqa' al-talaq bi al-rijal' — the pronouncement of divorce is by men, not the count. Thus, the hadith does not settle the number of divorces.
This linguistic restriction is key to the Hanafi rebuttal.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: critique of the hadith's chain
The author points out that the hadith is mawquf (stopped at a Companion), not marfu' (raised to the Prophet). According to Shafi'i usul, a mawquf hadith is not valid as evidence. This undercuts the Shafi'i argument from within their own methodology.
Citations from Ibn Abi Shaybah, Tabarani, and Abd al-Razzaq show the hadith's chain is mawquf.
5
Determine the practical difference and the tarjih
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): In the Hanafi school, a slave woman divorced twice becomes haram for her husband until she marries another; the Shafi'i school would allow a third divorce if the husband is free. The author favors the Hanafi position based on stronger qiyas and textual criticism.
The tarjih is clearly in favor of the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہوں گے جنس امتہ کی طلاق دو ہیں اور اس کی عدت دو حیض ہیں۔ اس حدیث سے ثابت ہوا کہ عد دطلاق میں عورتیں معتبر ہیں نہ کہ مرد۔ احناف کی دلیل عقلی یہ ہے کہ عورت کا محل نکاح ہو کر حلال ہونا اسکے حق میں نعمت ہے کیونکہ اسکی وجہ سے وہ نفقہ کسوہ اور سکنی وغیرہ کی مستحق ہوگی اور نعمتوں کو آدھا کرنے میں رقیت کو دخل ہے۔ پس قیاس کا تقاضا تو یہ تھا کہ باندی کو صرف ڈیڑھ طلاق دینا کافی ہوتا جو آزاد عورت کی طلاق کا نصف ہے ۔ مگر چونکہ طلاق میں تجزی اور تقسیم نہیں ہوتی اس آدھی طلاق کو پورا کر کے مکمل دو طلاقیں کر دی گئیں۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے امام شافعی کی ذکر کردہ حدیث کا جواب دیا ہے۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ الطلاق بالرجال کے معنی ہیں ایقاع الاطلاق بالرجال یعنی طلاق واقع کرنا مردوں کے اختیار میں ہے۔ اس تاویل کے بعد یہ حدیث شوافع کا مستدل نہ سکے گی۔ علامہ عینی نے فرمایا کہ ابن ابی شیبہ نے اپنی مصنف میں اس روایت کو ابن عباس پر موقوف کیا ہے۔ اور طبرانی نے اپنی معجم میں ابن سعد پر موقوف کرتے ہوئے روایت کیا ہے اور عبد الرزاق نے اپنے مصنف میں عثمان ، زید بن ثابت اور ابن عباس پر موقوف کرتے ہوئے روایت کیا ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ یہ حدیث مرفوع نہیں بلکہ موقوف ہے۔ اور شوافع کے نزدیک حدیث موقوف قابل استدلال نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ الہذا اس حدیث سے امام شافعی کا اپنے مذہب پر استدلال کرنا درست نہیں ہوگا۔
There will be... the slave woman's divorce is two, and her waiting period is two menses. This hadith proves that in counting divorce, women are considered, not men. The Hanafi rational proof is that for a woman to become a place of marriage and lawful is a blessing for her because she becomes entitled to maintenance, clothing, and housing, etc., and slavery has a role in cutting blessings in half. Therefore, analogical reasoning required that a slave woman be given only one and a half divorces, which is half of a free woman's divorce. But since divorce cannot be divided or split, that half divorce is completed to make two full divorces. The author of Al-Hidayah responded to the hadith mentioned by Imam al-Shafi'i. He says: the meaning of 'al-talaq bi al-rijal' is 'iqa' al-talaq bi al-rijal', meaning the pronouncing of divorce is in the hands of men. With this interpretation, this hadith cannot be used as evidence by the Shafi'is. Allamah Ayni said that Ibn Abi Shaybah in his Musannaf reported this narration as mawquf on Ibn Abbas, and Tabarani in his Mu'jam reported it as mawquf on Ibn Sa'd, and Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf reported it as mawquf on Uthman, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn Abbas. The conclusion is that this hadith is not marfu' but rather mawquf. According to the Shafi'is, a mawquf hadith is not valid as evidence. Therefore, it is not correct for Imam al-Shafi'i to use this hadith to support his madhhab.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the number of divorces that can be given to a slave woman. The Hanafi position is that a slave woman's divorce count is two, based on the general principle that the woman's own status (free or slave) determines the count, not the husband's. The author first gives the Hanafi rational proof: slavery halves blessings, so a slave woman's divorce count should be half of a free woman's (three). Since divorce is indivisible, it is completed to two. Then the author addresses the Shafi'i evidence, which is a hadith stating 'al-talaq bi al-rijal' (divorce is by men). The Hanafi response interprets this hadith to mean that the pronouncement of divorce is by men, not the count. Additionally, the author cites Allamah Ayni to show that this hadith is mawquf (stopped at a Companion) and therefore not acceptable as evidence according to the Shafi'i usul themselves, thus nullifying the Shafi'i argument.

Key Terms
الطلاق بالرجال (al-talaq bi al-rijal): A hadith used by the Shafi'is, interpreted by the Hanafis to mean the pronouncing of divorce is by men, not the count.
حدیث موقوف (hadith mawquf): A hadith that is only traced back to a Companion, not to the Prophet Muhammad.
ایقاع الاطلاق (iqa' al-talaq): The act of pronouncing or effecting divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Slave's Right to Divorce
Ruling that a slave's divorce is valid and master cannot divorce his wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's divorce and explain why the master cannot divorce the slave's wife.

Core Idea
The slave who marries with permission owns the marriage contract; only he can dissolve it.

This lesson addresses a clear and important rule: when a slave marries with his master's permission, the marriage contract belongs to the slave, not the master. Consequently, only the slave can divorce his wife. Many students mistakenly think the master, as owner of the slave, has authority over the marriage. Let's examine the precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the act: a slave divorces his wife after marrying with his master's permission. The ruling: the divorce takes effect (sahih).
The opposite case (marriage without permission) is not covered here.
2
Understand why the master's divorce is invalid
The passage explicitly states that the master's divorce does not take effect. The reason is that the marriage contract is the slave's right (milk al-nikah).
Ownership of the contract gives the right to nullify it (isqat).
3
Grasp the underlying principle
The legal principle is: whoever holds the right over a contract holds the right to dissolve it. Here, the slave holds the marriage contract, so only he can divorce.
This principle applies generally in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply to a simple scenario
If a slave named Zayd marries Hind with his master's permission, and later the master says 'I divorce you from Zayd,' that pronouncement has no effect. Only Zayd can divorce Hind.
No additional conditions are mentioned; the rule is straightforward.

The master has no authority to divorce the slave's wife. The evidence is given: the marriage contract is the slave's right, so the right to dissolve it (isqat) also belongs to him. This is a straightforward ruling with its reasoning.

اسقاط (isqat): The act of nullifying or canceling, here referring to divorce as nullification of the marriage.

Classification of Divorce: Explicit (Sarih) and Figurative (Kinayah)
Definition and rulings of explicit and figurative divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih and kinayah divorce, state the Hanafi ruling for each, and explain how intention affects the outcome in specific cases.

Core Idea
Sarih words need no intention; kinayah does. Intention only matters if the words can bear the intended meaning.

Divorce in Islamic law is not a single act but has various types. One key distinction is between explicit words (sarih) that are exclusively used for divorce and figurative words (kinayah) that can imply other meanings. This determines whether the husband's intention is needed and what the effect is. In this lesson, we explore the definitions, the default ruling of revocability, and the role of intention in different scenarios as per the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Define sarih and kinayah
From the text, identify the defining characteristic of sarih words (used exclusively for divorce) and kinayah words (used for divorce and other meanings). Note examples: 'anti taliq', 'anti muttalaqa', 'tallaqtuki' for sarih.
Sarih words are unambiguous; kinayah words require intention to specify divorce.
2
State the default ruling for sarih
Sarih words effect a revocable (raj'i) divorce without need for intention, as per the Quranic text and common usage.
Revocability means the husband may take her back during the waiting period.
3
Examine the effect of different intentions
The text covers three intentions: separation (ibanah), release from shackles (an withaq), and release from action (an al-amal). For each, note whether the divorce is considered in court (qada) and between him and Allah (diyana).
Intention of separation is thrown back; release from shackles is accepted diyanatan; release from action is not accepted at all by the majority.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Hanifa holds that intention of release from action is accepted diyanatan because the word can be used for liberation. The majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is not accepted at all.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The principle: intention is only considered if the words can bear the intended meaning. Sarih words cannot bear meanings contrary to their apparent sense unless the meaning is still within the scope of the word (like 'release from shackles' being a possible interpretation).
This principle governs many fiqh rulings beyond divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ايقاع الطلاق
ترجمہ (یہ) باب طلاق واقع کرنے کے ( بیان میں ) ہے
تشریح چونکہ گذشتہ باب میں مصنف نے اصل طلاق اور سنی اور بدعی ہونے کے اعتبار سے وصف طلاق کو بیان کیا ہے ۔ اسلئے اب اس باب میں من حیث الایقاع طلاق کے انواع و اقسام کو بیان فرمائیں گے۔
طلاق کی دو قسمیں ، طلاق صریح و کنایہ
الطلاق على ضربين صريح وكناية فالصريح قوله انت طالق ومطلقة و طلقتك فهذا يقع به الطلاق الرجعي لان هذه الالفاظ تستعمل فى الطلاق ولاتستعمل فى غيره فكان صريحا وانه يعقب الرجعة بالنص ولا يفتقر الى النية لانه صريح فيه لغلبة الاستعمال وكذا اذانوى الابانة لانه قصد تنجيز ما علقه الشرع بانقضاء العدة فيرد عليه ولونوى الطلاق عن وثاق لم يدين فى القضاء لانه خلاف الظاهر ويدين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لانه يحتمله ولونوى به الطلاق عن العمل لم يدين فى القضاء ولا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لان الطلاق لرفع القيد و هو غير مقيد بالعمل وعن ابي حنيفة انه يدين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لانه يستعمل للتخليص
ترجمہ طلاق دو قسم پر ہے صریح اور کنایہ۔ پس صریح اس کا قول انت طالق اور مطابقت اور طلقتک پس یہ ایسے الفاظ ہیں ) جن کے ساتھ طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ یہ الفاظ طلاق میں مستقل ہوتے ہیں اور غیر طلاق میں مستعمل نہیں ہوتے تو طلاق صریح ٹھہری اور صریح طلاق کے ) بعد رجعت ہوتی ہے۔ (بدلیل) نص اور ( طلاق صریح) محتاج نیت نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس نے اس چیز کو بالفعل کر دینے کا ارادہ کیا ہے جس کو شریعت نے عدت گزر نے پر معلق کیا ہے۔ لہذا اس کا ارادہ اس پر لوٹا دیا جائے گا۔ اور اگر اس نے بیڑی سے رہائی کا ارادہ کیا تو قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ اسلئے وہ خلاف ظاہر ہے اور فیما بینہ و بین اللہ تصدیق کر لی جائے گی ۔ اسلئے کہ لفظ اس کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ اور اگر عمل سے رہائی کی نیت کی تو نہ قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی اور نہ دیا یہ فیما بیند و بین اللہ ۔ اسلئے کہ طلاق رفع قید کیلئے ہوتی ہے اور وہ عورت عمل کے ساتھ مقید نہیں، اور ابو حنیفہ سے روایت ہے کہ فیما بیند و بین اللہ تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ یہ ( کلام ) چھٹکارا دینے میں استعمال کیا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح عبارت میں طلاق بمعنی تطلیق ہے۔ بہر حال طلاق کی دو قسمیں ہیں صریح اور کنایہ صریح اس کو کہتے ہیں جس کی مراد بغیر بیان کیئے ظاہر ہو جائے۔ اور کہنا یہ وہ ہے، جس کی مراد بغیر بیان کئے ظاہر نہ ہو سکے۔ صریحی طلاق یہ ہے کہ مرد اپنی بیوی سے کہے انت طالق ، انت مطلقة يا طلقتک ۔ ان الفاظ سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ الفاظ طلاق کے معنی میں استعمال ہوتے ہیں اور غیر طلاق میں استعمال نہیں ہوتے۔ لہذا یہ الفاظ طلاق کے معنی میں صریحی ہوں گے۔ اور طلاق صریحی کے بعد آیت و بعولتهن احق بردهن سے رجعت ثابت ہے اور طلاق صریحی محتاج نیت بھی نہیں ہے ۔ کیونکہ طلاق کے معنی میں یہ الفاظ غلبہ استعمال کی وجہ سے صریحی ہیں۔ حاصل یہ کہ نیت ہوتی ہے مہم کو متعین کرنے میں اور ان الفاظ کی دلالت میں کوئی ابہام نہیں ہے۔ لہذا یہ الفاظ اپنے معنی پر دلالت میں نیت کے محتاج نہیں ہوں گے۔ اسی طرح طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی اگر شوہر نے الفاظ صریحی سے بائنہ کرنے کی نیت کی ہو دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے اس چیز کو بالفعل کرنے کا ارادہ کیا جس کو شریعت نے عدت گزرنے پر معلق کیا تھا۔ یعنی شریعت نے طلاق صریح میں بینونت کا اعتبار عدت گذرنے سے بعد کیا تھا اور اس نے اعتبار کیا ہے کہ فی الحال بینونت حاصل ہو جائے۔ پس اس کے قصد اور اعتبار کو شریعت کے خلاف ہونے کی وجہ سے الٹا اس کے منہ پر پھینک دیا جائیگا۔ اور اگر اس نے انت طالق بول کر بیوی سے رہائی کی نیت کی تو حکم یہ ہے کہ قصبا تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی البتہ دیا یہ تصدیق کرلی جائیگی۔ اس بارے میں اصل یہ ہے کہ اگر کلام کسی معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لیکن وہ معنی خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ پس اگر متکلم اپنے اس کلام سے اس معنی کی نیت کرے تو خالف ظاہر ہونے کی وجہ سے قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی مگر چونکہ کلام اس معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے اس وجہ سے دیانہ فیمابینہ و بین اللہ تصدیق کر لی جائیگی۔ اور اگر انت طالق سے طلاق عن العمل (عمل سے رہائی) کی نیت کی ہے تو نہ قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی اور نہ دیا ہے ۔ کیونکہ اس کا کلام انت طالق طلاق عن العمل کا احتمال ہی نہیں رکھتا ہے۔ پس کلام جس معنی کا حتمال نہیں رکھتا اس معنی کی نیت معتبر نہیں ہو
Chapter of Effecting Divorce. Translation: This chapter is about effecting divorce. Explanation: Since in the previous chapter the author described the original divorce and its quality in terms of being sunni or bid'i, now in this chapter he will describe the types and kinds of divorce in terms of effecting it. Divorce is of two types: explicit (sarih) and figurative (kinayah). Explicit: his saying 'anti taliq', 'muttalaqa', or 'tallaqtuki'. With these, a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs because these words are used specifically for divorce and not used for anything else, so they are explicit. And it is followed by the possibility of return (ruj'ah) by text, and it does not need intention (niyyah) because it is explicit due to common usage. Similarly if he intends separation (ibanah) because he intended to actualize what the law made contingent upon the completion of the waiting period, so it is thrown back upon him. And if he intends release from shackles (talaq an withaq), it is not accepted in the court because it is contrary to the apparent meaning, but it is accepted between him and Allah because the words can bear it. And if he intends release from action (talaq an al-amal), it is not accepted in the court nor between him and Allah because divorce is for lifting a restriction, and she is not restricted by action. And from Abu Hanifa: it is accepted between him and Allah because it is used for liberation. Translation: Divorce is of two types: sarih and kinayah. Sarih is his saying 'anti taliq', 'muttalaqa', or 'tallaqtuki'. With these words, revocable divorce occurs. Because these words are used for divorce and are not used for other than it, so it is sarih. After sarih divorce, return is proven by the text. Sarih divorce does not need intention because it is explicit due to common usage. Similarly if he intends separation, because he intended to actualize what the law made contingent on completion of the waiting period, so his intention is thrown back. And if he intends release from shackles, it is not accepted in the court because it is against the apparent meaning, but it is accepted between him and Allah because the word can bear it. And if he intends release from action, it is not accepted in the court nor between him and Allah because divorce is for lifting a restriction, and she is not restricted by action. And from Abu Hanifa: it is accepted between him and Allah because it is used for liberation. Explanation: In the text, talaq means tathiq (divorce). In any case, divorce is of two types: sarih and kinayah. Sarih is that whose meaning becomes apparent without explanation. Kinayah is that whose meaning does not become apparent without explanation. Sarih divorce is when a man says to his wife 'anti taliq', 'anti muttalaqa', or 'tallaqtuki'. With these words, revocable divorce occurs. The evidence is that these words are used for divorce and not used for other than divorce, so they are sarih in meaning. After sarih divorce, return is proven by the verse 'wa bu'ulatuhunna ahaqqu biraddihinna'. And sarih divorce does not need intention because these words are explicit in the meaning of divorce due to common usage. The result is that intention is used to specify ambiguous things, and in these words there is no ambiguity, so they do not need intention to indicate their meaning. Similarly, revocable divorce occurs even if the husband intends irrevocable separation, because he intended to actualize what the law made contingent on completion of the waiting period; the law considered separation in sarih divorce after the waiting period, but he intended it immediately. Since his intention contradicts the law, it is thrown back on him. And if he says 'anti taliq' and intends release from the wife, the ruling is that it is not accepted in the court, but it is accepted between him and Allah. The principle is that if a word can bear a meaning but that meaning is contrary to the apparent meaning, then if the speaker intends that meaning, it is not accepted in court because it is against the apparent, but it is accepted between him and Allah because the word can bear it. And if he intends release from action (talaq an al-amal) with 'anti taliq', it is not accepted in court nor between him and Allah, because his statement 'anti taliq' does not bear the meaning of release from action. So an intended meaning that the words cannot bear is not valid.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the two types of divorce: explicit (sarih) and figurative (kinayah). The author first presents the Arabic matn from Al-Hidayah, then provides an Urdu translation and commentary. Explicit divorce uses specific words (anti taliq, anti muttalaqa, tallaqtuki) that are exclusively used for divorce; therefore, they effect a revocable divorce without need for intention. The ruling is supported by the Quranic verse permitting return during the waiting period. The text then details the effect of different intentions: if the husband intends irrevocable separation, it is still treated as revocable because the law only makes it irrevocable after the waiting period. If he intends release from shackles (an withaq), it is not accepted in court (because it is contrary to the apparent meaning) but is accepted between him and Allah. If he intends release from action (an al-amal), it is not accepted at all because the words do not imply such a meaning. Abu Hanifa reportedly held that it is accepted between him and Allah even in the latter case because the word can be used for liberation. The commentary clarifies the definitions and the principle: intention matters only when the words can bear the intended meaning.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Words that are exclusively used for divorce and do not require intention to effect divorce.
کنایہ (kinayah): Words that can imply divorce but are also used for other meanings; they require intention to effect divorce.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife without a new contract during the waiting period.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'anti taliq' to his wife intending release from shackles (talaq an withaq), what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is accepted in both court and between him and Allah.
B) It is not accepted in court but is accepted between him and Allah.
C) It is accepted in court but not between him and Allah.
D) It is not accepted at all.

Key Takeaway
Sarih divorce words need no intention and effect a revocable divorce; intention only matters diyanatan if the words can bear the intended meaning, and the majority holds that release from action is not a valid intention.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Sarih and Kinayah Talaq: Definitions, Automatic Rulings, and Intention
Defines sarih and kinayah talaq and explains the role of intention in court and before Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sarih from kinayah talaq, state the automatic revocable effect of sarih words, and evaluate the effect of contrary intentions in qada and diyanatan according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Sarih talaq words cause revocable divorce automatically; intention only matters diyanatan if the words can bear that meaning.

This passage from Al-Hidayah tackles a fundamental question in family law: what happens when a husband uses explicit words of divorce but claims a different intention? The text distinguishes between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (allusive) words, and then carefully unpacks how the Hanafi school treats contrary intentions in a court of law (qada) versus before Allah (diyanatan). Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone who advises on marital matters or wishes to avoid unintended legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and the sarih words
Locate the passage's opening division of talaq into sarih and kinayah. Note the three specific words listed as sarih: 'anti taliq', 'anti mutallaqah', 'tallaqtuk'.
These words are considered sarih because they are exclusively used for divorce in Arabic.
2
Understand the automatic ruling and its proof
The text states that uttering these words results in a revocable (raj'i) divorce without needing intention. Identify the Quranic proof (2:228) and the reasoning: the words are unambiguous, so intention is irrelevant for the legal effect.
The ruling is that sarih talaq is automatically raj'i even if the husband intends bainunah (irrevocability).
3
Analyse the qada/diyanatan distinction for contrary intentions
When the husband intends a meaning other than divorce (e.g. release from a chain), the text distinguishes between court judgment (qada) and divine judgment (diyanatan). In court, the apparent meaning prevails; diyanatan, if the word can bear the intended meaning, he is believed.
The key principle: intention is considered only if the words can linguistically support that meaning.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on release from work
The majority (Sahibayn) hold that 'anti taliq' cannot mean release from work, so the intention is invalid even diyanatan. Abu Hanifa has a narration accepting it diyanatan because 'talaq' is used for general release. Recognise which position is mufta bihi.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn.

جائیگی۔ اس بارے میں اصل یہ ہے کہ اگر کلام کسی معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لیکن وہ معنی خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ پس اگر متکلم اپنے اس کلام سے اس معنی کی نیت کرے تو خالف ظاہر ہونے کی وجہ سے قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی مگر چونکہ کلام اس معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے اس وجہ سے دیانہ فیمابینہ و بین اللہ تصدیق کر لی جائیگی۔ اور اگر انت طالق سے طلاق عن العمل (عمل سے رہائی) کی نیت کی ہے تو نہ قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی اور نہ دیا ہے ۔ کیونکہ اس کا کلام انت طالق طلاق عن العمل کا احتمال ہی نہیں رکھتا ہے۔ پس کلام جس معنی کا حتمال نہیں رکھتا اس معنی کی نیت معتبر نہیں ہوتی ۔ جیسے کوئی انت طالق کہہ کر انت آکلہ مراد لے تو اس کی نیست معتبر نہیں ہوتی ۔ اور رہی یہ بات کہ انت طالق طلاق عن العمل کا احتمال کیوں نہیں رکھتا تو اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ طلاق قید نکاح کو اٹھانے کیلئے ہوتی ہے اور قید نکاح غیر مقید باعمل ہے لہذ اطلاق قید بالعمل کو اٹھانے کیلئے نہیں ہوگی ۔ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایک روایت یہ ہے کہ دیا نہ طلاق عن العمل کے معنی کی تصدیق کی جائے گی دلیل یہ ہے کہ طلاق کا لفظ چھٹکارا دینے کے معنی میں مستعمل ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا انت طالق کے معنی انت مخلصةً عن العمل کے ہوں گے۔
Divorce is of two types: explicit (sarih) and allusive (kinayah). Explicit divorce is when one says 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqah', or 'tallaqtuk'. With these words, revocable divorce will occur, because these words are specific to divorce and not used for other than divorce, so it is explicit divorce. After explicit divorce, reconciliation (raj'ah) occurs, by textual proof (nass). Explicit divorce does not require intention, because he intended to do that which the Shari'ah made conditional upon completion of the waiting period; thus his intention will be turned back upon him. If he intended release from a chain, it will not be believed in the court because it is contrary to what is apparent, but it will be believed between him and Allah, because the word can bear that meaning. If he intended release from work, it will not be believed in the court nor between him and Allah, because divorce is for removing the shackle (of marriage), and the woman is not shackled by work. It is reported from Abu Hanifa that he will be believed between him and Allah, because the word is used for release. Commentary: In the phrase, 'talaq' means 'tatliq'. Anyway, divorce is of two types: sarih and kinayah. Sarih is that whose meaning is clear without explanation; kinayah is that whose meaning is not clear without explanation. Sarih divorce is when a man says to his wife 'anti taliq', 'anti mutallaqah', or 'tallaqtuk'. With these words, revocable divorce occurs. The proof is that these words are used in the meaning of divorce and not used for other than divorce, so they are explicit. After sarih divorce, reconciliation is proven by the verse 'And their husbands are more entitled to take them back' (Quran 2:228). Sarih divorce also does not need intention, because these words are explicit due to prevalent usage. The conclusion: intention is for specifying ambiguity, and there is no ambiguity in the indication of these words, so they do not need intention for indication. Similarly, if the husband intended irrevocability (bainunah) with these explicit words, revocable divorce still occurs, because he intended to bring about something that the Shari'ah postponed until after the waiting period; his intention is rejected as contrary. If he said 'anti taliq' intending release from the wife, the ruling is that it is not accepted in court but is accepted between him and Allah. The principle: if the speech can bear a meaning contrary to the apparent, the court does not accept it, but diyanatan it is accepted. If he intends 'talaq 'an al-'amal' (release from work), it is not accepted in court nor diyanatan, because his speech cannot bear that meaning; intention for a meaning the speech cannot bear is invalid. Similarly, if one says 'anti taliq' intending 'you are an eater', that intention is not valid. The reason it cannot bear release from work is that divorce is for removing the marriage bond, and the marriage bond is not tied to work; thus release from work is not intended. There is a report from Abu Hanifa that diyanatan he will be believed, because the word 'talaq' is used for release, so 'anti taliq' would mean 'you are released from work.'
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the definition of sarih and kinayah talaq and the effect of intention. The text begins by stating that divorce is of two types: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (allusive). Sarih talaq is performed using specific words: 'anti taliq', 'anti mutallaqah', or 'tallaqtuk'. These words are considered sarih because they are exclusively used for divorce in the Arabic language; they are not used for other meanings. As a result, uttering them results in a revocable (raj'i) divorce automatically, without any need for the husband's intention. The proof for the revocability is the Quranic verse 'wa bu'ulatuhunna ahaqqu biraddihinna' (2:228), which establishes the right of return during the iddah period. The text then explains that if the husband uses these sarih words but intends something other than divorce, such as 'release from a chain' (wathaq), the ruling differs between worldly courts (qada) and the hereafter (diyanatan). In a court of law, the husband's claim will not be accepted because the apparent meaning of his words is divorce, and the court judges by outward appearances. However, between him and Allah, he is believed, because the word 'taliq' can linguistically bear the meaning of release. If he intends 'release from work' (amal), the matter is more restricted: even diyanatan his intention is not accepted, according to the majority of Hanafi scholars. The reasoning is that divorce is specifically for removing the marriage bond (qayd al-nikah), and the marriage bond is not tied to work. Therefore, the word cannot possibly mean release from work. Imam Abu Hanifa has a narration that even for release from work, the intention is accepted diyanatan, because 'talaq' is used for general release in Arabic language. The commentary (tashreeh) elaborates: sarih is that whose intended meaning is clear without explanation; kinayah requires explanation. The three words are sarih because they are predominantly used for divorce. The principle is that intention is needed to specify ambiguous words; since these words are unambiguous, intention is irrelevant. When a husband intends irrevocability (bainunah) immediately, the law rejects that because the Shari'ah has made bainunah conditional upon completion of the iddah; thus the divorce remains raj'i. Then the text discusses the intention of release from the wife herself: this is a possible but contrary meaning, so the court does not accept it, but it is accepted diyanatan. The final part clarifies that for release from work, the word cannot bear that meaning, except according to Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Words that are exclusively used for divorce and whose meaning is clear without explanation.
کنایہ (kinayah): Words that are not exclusively used for divorce and require intention or context to indicate divorce.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract.
قضاء (qada): The court's ruling based on outward appearances (zahir).
دیانتاً (diyanatan): Matters as they are accepted between the person and Allah, based on inner truth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti taliq' intending release from a chain (wathaq), what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the divorce?
A) The divorce is invalid both in court and before Allah.
B) The divorce is valid in court but not before Allah.
C) The divorce is not valid in court but valid before Allah.
D) The divorce is valid in both court and before Allah.

Key Takeaway
Sarih talaq words are unambiguous in court; only meanings the words can possibly bear are accepted diyanatan, and the majority Hanafi position restricts acceptablity to meanings directly related to release from marriage.

The Word 'Mutallaqah' and Its Ruling on Divorce
Ruling on the word 'mutallaqah' as kinayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the word 'mutallaqah' and distinguish why it is considered kinayah (allusive) rather than sarih (explicit) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The word 'mutallaqah' does not effect divorce by itself — intention is required because it is not customarily used for divorce.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text clarifies the legal effect of a specific word, 'mutallaqah' (with sukoon on ta). While it may resemble sarih words of divorce, the Hanafi school classifies it as kinayah due to customary usage. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining when a divorce actually occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its pronunciation
The word is 'mutallaqah' with sukoon on ta and fatha on lam. This pronunciation distinguishes it from other forms like 'taliq'.
The pronunciation matters; the original Arabic specifies 'with sukoon on the ta'.
2
Determine its customary usage
Check if this word is used in everyday language to mean divorce. The text states it is not customarily ('urfan) used for divorce.
'Urf is a key factor in Hanafi classification of sarih vs kinayah.
3
Apply the sarih/kinayah classification
Since it is not customarily used for divorce, it is not sarih; it is kinayah. Therefore, intention (niyyah) is required for divorce to occur.
Sarih terms do not require intention; kinayah terms do.
4
State the ruling
Without intention, no divorce occurs. With intention, one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) takes place.
The text clarifies that only one divorce occurs even if the husband intended more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال انتِ مُطلقة بتسكين الطاء لا يكون طلاقا الابالنية لانها غير مستعملة فيه عرفا فلم يكن صريحا ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت مطلقہ طا کو سکون دے کر تو ( یہ ) طلاق نہیں ہوگی۔ مگر نیت کے ساتھ ۔ کیونکہ یہ لفظ معنی طلاق میں عرفا مستعمل نہیں ہے۔ پس یہ لفظ طلاق میں ) صریحی نہ ہوا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی منکوحہ بیوی سے است مطلقہ کہا یعنی طا ء کا سکون اور لام کا فتحہ تو اس سے بلائیت طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ البتہ بیت طلاق ، طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں عرفا مستعمل نہیں لہذا یہ لفظ صریحی طلاق نہیں ہوگا۔ مگر چونکہ اس لفظ سے مقصود طلاق اداء ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسلئے اگر طلاق کی نیت پائی گئی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائیگی۔ مذکورہ لفظ سے ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی اگر چہ زیادہ کی نیت بھی ہو،
And if he says 'anti mutallaqah' with sukoon on the ta, it is not talaq except with intention, because it is not customarily used in the meaning of talaq, so it is not sarih. Translation: And if he said 'anti mutallaqah' with sukoon on ta, then it is not talaq except with intention, because this word is not customarily used in the meaning of talaq, so it is not sarih. Commentary: The issue is that if a person said to his wife 'anti mutallaqah' - meaning sukoon on ta and fatha on lam - then without intention talaq does not occur. However, if there is intention of talaq, talaq will occur. The proof is that this word is not customarily used in the meaning of talaq, so it is not sarih. But because the word can be used to denote talaq, if intention is found, talaq occurs. With this word, only one talaq occurs, even if he intended more than that.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the word 'mutallaqah'. The text teaches that if a husband says to his wife 'anti mutallaqah' with the correct pronunciation (sukoon on the letter ta and fatha on lam), this is not an explicit (sarih) divorce word. Therefore, no divorce takes effect by this word alone - intention is required. The reason is that this word is not customarily ('urfan) used in the Arabic language to mean divorce. However, the word can be intended for divorce, so if the husband has the intention of divorcing his wife, then one revocable divorce occurs. The commentary adds that if he intends more than one divorce (e.g., three), only one takes place. This is a specific ruling for this particular word, which is a form of praise (like 'free woman') but can be used for divorce with proper intention. Key point: This word is kinayah (allusive), not sarih.

Key Terms
مطلقہ (mutallaqah): A word meaning 'divorced woman' or 'freed woman'; here specifically with sukoon on ta, it is not a sarih term for divorce.
عرفاً (urfan): According to customary usage; what is commonly understood by speakers of the language.

The Hanafi View on Number of Divorces with 'Taliq'
Presents inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on number of divorces with 'taliq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that only one divorce occurs with sarih words like 'taliq', and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
When 'taliq' is used, only one divorce occurs — it is an adjective for a single woman and cannot convey multiplicity.

In the Hanafi school, the word 'taliq' and its synonyms are considered sarih (explicit) words that effect a revocable divorce. However, a question arises: if the husband intends more than one divorce, do multiple divorces occur? This lesson examines the Hanafi position and the Shafi'i disagreement, focusing on the grammatical reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: with each sarih word (talaq, anti taliq, etc.), only one divorce occurs regardless of the husband's intention.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the word 'taliq' is a mention of divorce in language, so numbers can attach to it, and whatever number is intended will take effect.
He analogizes 'taliq' to 'alim' (knowledge) where numbers are valid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic proof
Hanafis argue that 'taliq' is an adjective (na't) for a single female, as evidenced by its dual and plural forms (taliqan, tawaliq). As a singular adjective, it cannot inherently bear number.
Number (plurality) is considered the opposite of 'fard' (singular).
4
Understand the Hanafi response to number attachment
When a number is attached, it is actually an adjective for an implied infinitive (masdar), not for the word 'taliq' itself. The phrase 'taliq thalath' means 'talaqan thalatha' (three divorces) but the word 'taliq' still refers to one.
This is similar to saying 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarat al-khilaf)
The difference: In a Hanafi court, saying 'anti taliq thalath' results in one revocable divorce; in a Shafi'i court, it would result in three divorces.
This has significant implications for the permissibility of remarriage and the number of divorces remaining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولايقع به الاواحدة وان نوى اكثر من ذلك وقال الشافعي يقع مانوی لانه محتمل لفظه فان ذكر الطالق ذكر للطلاق لغة كذكر العالم ذكر للعلم ولهذا يصح قرآن العددبه فيكون نصبا على التفسير ولنا انه نعت فرد حتى قيل للمثنى طالقان وللثلاث طوالق فلا يحتمل العدد لانه ضده وذكر الطالق ذكر لطلاق هو صفة للمرأة لالطلاق هو تطليق والعدد الذى يقترن به نعت لمصدر محذوف معناه طلاقا ثلثا كقولك اعطيته جزیلا ای اعطاء جزيلا ترجمہ اور نہیں واقع ہوگی (مذکورہ تین لفظوں میں سے ہر ایک کے ساتھ مگر ایک (طلاق ) اگر چہ اس سے زائد کی نیت کی ہو۔ اور فرمایا امام شافعی نے کہ جسکی نیت کی ہے وہ واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ مانوی اسکے لفظ کا عمل ہے۔ اسلئے کہ طالق کا ذکر لغہ طلاق کا ذکر ہے۔ جیسا کہ عالم کا ذکر کر نا علم کا ذکر کرتا ہے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے اس کے ساتھ عدد کا متصل کرنا صحیح ہے۔ پس ہوگا (لفظ ثلاثا ) منصوب تمیز کی بناء پر ۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہیکہ لفظ طالق) نعت فرد ہے حتی کہ دو کیلئے طالقان اور تین کیلئے طوالق کہا جاتا ہے۔ پس عدد کا احتمال نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ (فرد) اسکی ضد ہے اور طالق کا ذکر کرنا ایسی طلاق کا ذکر کرنا ہے جو عورت کی صفت ہے نہ کہ اس طلاق کا جو تعلیق (کے معنی میں ہے) اور وہ عدد جو اس کے ساتھ مفترق ہے مصدر محذوف کی صفت ہے۔ اسکے معنی ہیں طلاقاً ثلاثا جیسے تیرا قول اعطيته جزيلاً یعنی اعطاء جزيلا - تشریح. لا يقع به الا واحدة كا تعلق سابقہ عبارت فهذا يقع به الطلاق الرجعی کے ساتھ ہے۔ اب حاصل مسئلہ یہ ہوگا کہ الفاظ طلاق ، انت طالق ، مطلقہ طلقتک کے ذریعہ طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی ۔ اور ان الفاظ ثلاثہ میں ہر ایک کے ساتھ صرف ایک طلاق واقع
He (the author) said: And (with each of the three words) only one divorce occurs, even if he intended more than that. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: What he intended occurs, because his word is capable of it. For the mention of 'taliq' is a mention of divorce in language, just as the mention of 'alim' is a mention of knowledge. For this reason it is permissible to attach a number to it, and it becomes an accusative of specification (tamyiz). Our proof is that 'taliq' is an adjective for a single female, such that for two it is said 'taliqan' and for three 'tawaliq'. So it cannot bear number, because number is its opposite. The mention of 'taliq' is a mention of divorce that is an attribute of the woman, not of divorce as an action (tatliq). The number that accompanies it is an adjective for a deleted infinitive, meaning 'talaqan thalatha' just as you say 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'. Translation: And (with each of the three words) only one (divorce) will occur, even if he intended more. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: What he intended will occur, because his intention is an action of his word. For the mention of 'taliq' is a mention of divorce in language, like the mention of 'alim' is a mention of knowledge. For this reason it is valid to attach a number to it, and it becomes an accusative of specification. And our proof is that 'taliq' is an adjective for a single female, such that for dual 'taliqan' and for plural 'tawaliq' is said. So it cannot bear number, because number is its opposite. The mention of 'taliq' is a mention of divorce that is an attribute of the woman, not of divorce as an action. The number that accompanies it is an adjective for a deleted infinitive, meaning 'talaqan thalatha' like your saying 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'. Commentary: The statement 'only one occurs' is connected to the previous statement 'with these words revocable divorce occurs'. Now the conclusion is: with the words 'talaq', 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqah', 'tallaqtuk', revocable divorce will occur, and with each of these three words only one divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the number of divorces that take effect when using the word 'taliq' (or its variants). The text begins by stating the Hanafi position: with each of the three sarih words (talaq, anti taliq, tallahtuk), only one divorce occurs, even if the husband intended more (e.g., three). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that if the husband intends multiple divorces, all of them occur, because the word 'taliq' is linguistically a mention of divorce, similar to how 'alim' mentions knowledge. Therefore, one can attach numbers to it, like saying 'taliq thalath' meaning three divorces. The Hanafi response is that 'taliq' is an adjective (na't) for a single feminine object; it has a dual form (taliqan) and a plural (tawaliq). As an adjective, it cannot simultaneously indicate a number because number (plurality) is its opposite. Moreover, when a number is attached, it is actually an adjective for an omitted infinitive (masdar), not for the word 'taliq'. For example, 'a'taytuhu jazilan' means 'i'ta'an jazilan' (a generous giving). Similarly, if one says 'taliq thalath', it means 'talaqan thalatha' (three divorces) but that does not change the fact that the word 'taliq' itself only indicates one. The commentary then connects to the prior ruling: these sarih words cause revocable divorce, and only one divorce occurs with each utterance regardless of the number intended. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
نعت فرد (na't fard): An adjective for a singular, referring to a single entity; it does not inherently indicate plurality.
تمیز (tamyiz): An accusative of specification in Arabic grammar that removes ambiguity from a preceding word or phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many divorces occur when a husband says 'anti taliq thalath' intending three divorces?
A) Three divorces
B) One revocable divorce
C) No divorce
D) One irrevocable divorce

Key Takeaway
When a sarih word like 'taliq' is used, only one divorce occurs — the intended number does not take effect because the word is grammatically singular.

The Word 'Mutallaqa' as Kinaya for Divorce
States that 'anti mutallaqa' is not sarih but kinaya requiring intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih and kinaya words for talaq and apply the rule that 'mutallaqa' requires intention.

Core Idea
A word not customarily used for divorce is kinaya: it effects divorce only with intention.

This lesson examines the Hanafi classification of the word 'mutallaqa' (with sukun on ta) in the context of divorce. While it derives from talaq, it is not among the sarih (explicit) words because it is not customarily used for that purpose. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining when divorce actually occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its pronunciation
The word 'mutallaqa' is pronounced with sukun on the ta and fatha on the lam. It resembles the word for 'divorced woman' but is not in common usage for divorce.
2
Determine its usage in custom (urf)
Hanafi fiqh relies on 'urf to decide if a word is sarih or kinaya. 'Mutallaqa' is not customarily used to pronounce divorce, so it falls under kinaya.
'Urf is the customary practice of the people.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for kinaya words
Kinaya words do not effect divorce without clear intention (niyyah). The speaker's state of mind is essential.
4
Conclude the ruling
If the husband intends divorce when saying 'mutallaqa', one revocable divorce occurs. Without intention, no divorce.
5
Note the consequence of intending more than one
Even if the husband intended two or three divorces, only one falls because the word is singular and kinaya does not accommodate number.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text

ولوقال انتِ مُطلقة بتسكين الطاء لا يكون طلاقا الابالنية لانها غير مستعملة فيه عرفا فلم يكن صريحا ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت مطلقہ طا کو سکون دے کر تو ( یہ ) طلاق نہیں ہوگی۔ مگر نیت کے ساتھ ۔ کیونکہ یہ لفظ معنی طلاق میں عرفا مستعمل نہیں ہے۔ پس یہ لفظ طلاق میں ) صریحی نہ ہوا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی منکوحہ بیوی سے انت مطلقہ کہا یعنی طا ء کا سکون اور لام کا فتحہ تو اس سے بلائیت طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ البتہ بیت طلاق ، طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ لفظ طلاق کے معنی میں عرفا مستعمل نہیں لہذا یہ لفظ صریحی طلاق نہیں ہوگا۔ مگر چونکہ اس لفظ سے مقصود طلاق اداء ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسلئے اگر طلاق کی نیت پائی گئی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائیگی۔ مذکورہ لفظ سے ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی اگر چہ زیادہ کی نیت بھی ہو،
And if he said, 'Anti mutallaqa' with sukun on the ta, it does not become divorce except with intention, because it is not customarily used in that meaning, so it is not sarih. Translation: And if he said 'anti mutallaqa' with sukun on the ta, then it will not be divorce except with intention, because this word is not customarily used in the meaning of divorce. So it is not sarih in divorce. Explanation: The issue is that if a person said to his married wife 'anti mutallaqa' meaning sukun on ta and fatha on lam, then without intention divorce will not occur. However, with intention of divorce, divorce will occur. The proof is that this word is not customarily used in the meaning of divorce, therefore this word is not sarih for divorce. But since the purpose of this word is to express divorce, therefore if intention of divorce is found, divorce will occur. From the mentioned word, one divorce will occur even if he intended more.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines the legal status of the word 'mutallaqa' (with sukun on ta) in divorce. It is not a sarih (explicit) word for divorce because it is not customarily used for that purpose. Therefore, it is considered kinaya (allusive) and requires intention for divorce to take effect. If the husband says this to his wife with the intention of divorce, one revocable divorce occurs. Even if he intended two or three, only one falls because the word is singular and does not accommodate number. The ruling is based on 'urf (custom) and the principle that kinaya words require niyyah.

Key Terms
مطلقہ (mutallaqa): The word 'mutallaqa' with sukun on ta means 'one who is divorced' but is not customarily used as a direct divorce pronouncement; it is considered kinaya.
صریح (sarih): Explicit words for divorce that effect divorce without intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'anti mutallaqa' (with sukun on ta) without any intention, does divorce occur?
A) Yes, because it is sarih.
B) No, because it is kinaya and requires intention.
C) Yes, one revocable divorce.
D) No, it is not a valid word for divorce.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mutallaqa' (with sukun on ta) is kinaya for divorce; it requires intention and yields only one divorce regardless of the number intended.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on Number of Divorces with 'Anti Taliq' and Similar Words
Presents Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on how many divorces fall with one utterance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that only one divorce occurs with 'anti taliq' and contrast this with the Shafi'i position, identifying the linguistic basis of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A singular adjective cannot bear number: 'anti taliq' means one divorce only.

In this passage, the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools debate a key linguistic question: does the word 'taliq' accommodate multiple divorces? The answer determines how many divorces fall when a husband says 'anti taliq' intending more than one. The Hanafi school insists on a strict grammatical reading, while the Shafi'i school looks at the underlying meaning. Understanding this dispute reveals how deeply grammar and usul al-lughah shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: with the words 'talaq', 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqa', only one revocable divorce occurs, regardless of intention.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Zufar, and one narration of Ahmad) holds that the number intended by the husband occurs, because the word can bear that number.
The Shafi'i view relies on the masdar implied in 'taliq'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: 'taliq' is derived from the masdar 'talaq', which can be general; therefore, attaching number (e.g., thalatha) is valid as tamyiz.
They compare to 'alim' implying 'ilm'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the masdar implied is the attribute of the woman (waqi' talaq), not the act of the husband (tatliq). Number attaches only to the act. The attached number is an adjective for a deleted masdar, not tamyiz.
Linguistic distinction: one type of talaq admits number, the other does not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under Hanafi, a husband who says 'anti taliq' intending three gets only one revocable divorce; under Shafi'i, he would get three divorces, ending the marriage irrevocably.
The difference affects reconciliation and the possibility of remarriage.

اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ طالق نعت فرد ہے کیونکہ یہ ایک عورت کی صفت ہے حتی کہ دو کیلئے طالقان اور تین کیلئے طوالق کہا جاتا ہے اور وہ لفظ جو نعت فرد ہوعدد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا کیونکہ عدد فرد کی ضد ہے اور شی ، اپنی ضد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ہے ۔ لہذا انت طالق سے دویا تین طلاق کی نیت کرنا درست نہیں۔

Imam al-Shafi'i's viewpoint: He said: 'Only one occurs, even if he intended more than that.' And al-Shafi'i said: 'What he intended occurs, because it is possible in his word.' Because mentioning 'taliq' is mentioning 'talaq' linguistically, just as mentioning 'alim' is mentioning 'ilm'. For this reason, attaching number to it is valid, so it becomes mansub as tamyiz. Our proof: it is a singular adjective, so for two 'taliqan' and for three 'tawaliq' is said. So it does not admit number because it is its opposite. And mentioning 'taliq' is mentioning a divorce that is an attribute of the woman, not the divorce that is 'tatliq'. And the number that accompanies it is an adjective for a deleted masdar, meaning 'talaqan thalatha', like your saying 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'. Translation: And it will not occur (with each of the three words except one, even if he intended more. And Imam al-Shafi'i said that what he intended occurs, because his intention is an action of his word. Because mentioning 'taliq' is mentioning 'talaq' linguistically, just as mentioning 'alim' is mentioning 'ilm'. And for this reason, attaching number to it is valid. So the word 'thalatha' becomes mansub as tamyiz. Our proof: the word 'taliq' is a singular adjective, so for two 'taliqan' and for three 'tawaliq' is said. So it does not admit number because number is its opposite. And mentioning 'taliq' is mentioning a divorce that is an attribute of the woman, not the divorce that is 'tatliq'. And the number that is coupled with it is an adjective for a deleted masdar, meaning 'talaqan thalatha', like your saying 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'. Explanation: 'la yaqa' bihi illa wahida' is connected to the previous phrase 'fahadha yaqa' bihi al-talaq al-rij'i'. Now the conclusion is that with the words 'talaq', 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqa', a revocable divorce occurs. And with each of these three words, only one divorce occurs, even if he intended more. This is our madhhab. And Imam al-Shafi'i's madhhab is that as many divorces as he intends, two or three, they occur accordingly. This is also the saying of Imam Malik, Imam Zufar, and in one narration of Imam Ahmad. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: that this person, whatever number he intends, one, two, or three, it is possible in his word, because mentioning the adjective form 'taliq' is mentioning the masdar 'talaq'. Because the adjective form cannot exist without its derived masdar. Just as saying the word 'alim' is mentioning 'ilm'. The conclusion is that the word 'taliq' contains the masdar, and the masdar by consensus admits one and more than one. For this reason, attaching number to the word 'taliq' is correct. Thus it is said 'anti taliq thalatha', and this word becomes mansub as tamyiz, and the purpose of tamyiz is to specify one of the contents of the word. So the summary is that the word 'taliq' admits the few and the many. Therefore, whatever he intends, two or three, he intends what his speech can bear, and intending what the speech can bear is valid in Shari'a. And our proof: the word 'taliq' is a singular adjective, because it is an attribute of one woman, so for two 'taliqan' and for three 'tawaliq' is said. And a word that is a singular adjective does not admit number, because number is its opposite, and a thing does not admit its opposite. Therefore, with 'anti taliq', intending two or three divorces is not valid. The answer to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: the word 'taliq' as an adjective form undoubtedly indicates the masdar 'talaq'. But this is the divorce that is an attribute of the woman (i.e., occurrence of divorce), not the divorce that is 'tatliq' and the act of the husband. And the admission of number is in the word 'talaq' that means 'tatliq', not in that which is an attribute of the woman. Therefore, deducing from mentioning 'taliq' to mentioning 'talaq' is not valid. And Imam al-Shafi'i's statement that number is attached to the word 'taliq': its answer is that in 'anti taliq thalatha', the number is an adjective for a deleted masdar, not a tamyiz. The implicit expression would be 'anti taliq talaqan thalatha'. This is like a person saying 'a'taytuhu jazilan' meaning 'i'ta'an jazilan'.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a full inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position: with the sarih words 'talaq', 'anti taliq', and 'mutallaqa', only one revocable divorce occurs, even if the husband intended two or three. The Shafi'i position: the number of divorces depends on the husband's intention. The Hanafi proof is linguistic: the word 'taliq' is a singular adjective (na't fard), so it cannot accommodate the dual or plural. The Shafi'i proof: 'taliq' implies the masdar 'talaq', which can be general and plural; hence number is valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes the masdar that is an attribute of the woman (waqi' talaq) from the masdar that is the husband's action (tatliq). Number attaches only to the latter. The Hanafi view is the relied-upon position in the madhhab.

Key Terms
طالق (taliq): The feminine active participle from talaq, used as an attribute of a woman: 'divorced'.
صریح (sarih): Explicit words that effect divorce without intention.
نعت فرد (na't fard): Singular adjective: a word that describes a single entity and does not accommodate the dual or plural in its singular form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that with sarih words like 'anti taliq', only one divorce occurs, because the word is a singular adjective that cannot bear number.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Number of Divorces in Sarih Phrases like 'Anti al-Talaq'
Explains that these phrases are sarih and the number of divorces depends on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for 'anti al-talaq' and similar phrases, and explain why intention of three divorces is valid but intention of two is not.

Core Idea
A singular masdar like 'talaq' implies the genus (three) but not a specific number like two.

In divorce, clarity of wording is crucial. Some phrases explicitly include the word 'talaq' (sarih). This lesson examines three such phrases and how many divorces occur based on intention. The key principle: the word 'talaq' as a masdar can indicate plurality but not the dual number.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sarih phrases and their effect
Recognize that 'anti al-talaq', 'anti taliq al-talaq', and 'anti taliq talaqan' are sarih; they effect divorce without intention.
The presence of the masdar 'talaq' makes them explicit.
2
Determine number of divorces for different intentions
Apply the rule: no intention or intending one or two gives one revocable divorce; intending three gives three.
Intention of two is not valid in the Hanafi view.
3
Analyze the linguistic basis for intention of three
The masdar 'talaq' is a genus noun; it can imply generality and plurality, so intention of three is valid.
Analogous to saying 'rajul 'adl' meaning a man of justice.
4
Compare with Imam Zufar's objection
Zufar argues by qiyas: since intention of three is valid, two should also be valid. The Hanafi response: validity of three is due to genus, not numerical admission; a singular word cannot accommodate the dual.
The dual is distinct from singular (individual or generic).

انت الطلاق يا انت طالق الطلاق یا انت طالق طلاق کے الفاظ سے کتنی طلاقیں واقع ہوں گی
ولو قال انت الطلاق أو انت طالق الطلاق اوانت طالق طلاقافان لم تكن له نية اونوى واحدة اوثنتين فهى واحدة رجعية وان نوى ثلثا فثلث ووقوع الطلاق باللفظة الثانية والثالثة ظاهر لانه لو ذكر النعت وحده يقع به الطلاق فاذا ذكره وذكر المصدر معه وانه يزيده وكادة اولى واما وقوعه باللفظة الاولى فان المصدريذكرو يراد به الاسم يقال رجل عدل اى عادل فصار بمنزلة قوله انت طالق وعلى هذا لو قال انت طلاق يقع الطلاق به ايضا ولا يحتاج فيه الى النية ويكون رجعيا لما بينا انه صريح الطلاق لغلبة الاستعمال فيه وتصح نية الثلث لان المصدر يحتمل العموم والكثرة لانه اسم جنس فيعتبر لسائر اسماء الاجناس فتتناول الادنى مع احتمال الكل ولا تصح نية الثنتين فيها خلافالزفر هو يقول ان الثنتين بعض الثلث فلما صحت نية الثلث صحت نية بعضها ضرورة ونحن نقول نية الثلث انما صحت لكونها جنسا حتى لو كانت المرأة امة تصح نية الثنتين باعتبار معنى الجنسية اما الثنتان في حق الحرة عددو اللفظ لا يحتمل العدد وهذا لان معنى التوحد مراعا فى الفاظ الوحدان وذلك بالفردية او الجنسية والمثنى بمعزل منهما
How many divorces occur with the words 'anti al-talaq', 'anti taliq al-talaq', or 'anti taliq talaqan'? And if he said 'anti al-talaq' or 'anti taliq al-talaq' or 'anti taliq talaqan', then if he had no intention, or intended one or two, it is one revocable divorce. And if he intended three, then three. And the occurrence of divorce with the second and third expressions is apparent, because if he mentioned the adjective alone, divorce occurs with it; so if he mentions it and mentions the masdar with it, that increases emphasis, so it is more appropriate. As for occurrence with the first expression: the masdar is mentioned and the noun is intended, as it is said 'rajul 'adl' meaning 'adil'. So it becomes like his saying 'anti taliq'. And on this, if he said 'anti talaq', divorce occurs with it as well, and it does not require intention, and it is revocable, as we have explained that it is sarih for divorce due to prevalence of usage. And intention of three is valid because the masdar admits generality and plurality, as it is a genus noun; it is considered like other genus nouns, so it includes the minimum while admitting the whole. And intention of two is not valid in it, contrary to Zufar. He says that two is a part of three; so when intention of three is valid, intention of its part must be valid. And we say: intention of three is valid only because it is a genus; even if the woman is a slave, intention of two is valid based on the meaning of genus. But as for two in the case of a free woman, it is a number, and the word does not admit number. This is because the meaning of oneness (tawahhud) is observed in singular words, either through individuality (fardiyya) or generality (jinsiyya), and the dual is separate from both.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage deals with phrases that explicitly include the word 'talaq' (or its masdar). These are sarih and do not require intention. The ruling: with 'anti al-talaq', 'anti taliq al-talaq', or 'anti taliq talaqan', if he had no intention or intended one or two, one revocable divorce occurs. If he intended three, three occur. The reasoning: 'anti al-talaq' is like 'anti taliq' (talaq as a noun meaning the act). 'Anti taliq al-talaq' and 'anti taliq talaqan' emphasize the masdar, so they are even stronger. Intention of three is valid because the masdar 'talaq' is a genus noun that includes the plural. However, intention of two is not valid, contrary to Imam Zufar. Zufar's argument that two is part of three is rejected because the word does not admit the specific number two; the singular form (whether individual or generic) cannot accommodate the dual.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): The noun form of divorce, used as a genus noun when saying 'anti al-talaq' meaning 'you are the divorce'.
مصدر (masdar): Verbal noun; 'talaq' is a masdar that can imply generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'anti al-talaq' intending two divorces, how many occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Sarih divorce phrases like 'anti al-talaq' effect one revocable divorce; intention of three is valid but intention of two is not.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner

The Hanafi Ruling on Three Divorce Phrases
Statement of ruling on three divorce phrases and basic conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for 'Anti al-talaq', 'Anti taliq al-talaq', and 'Anti taliq talaqan' with or without intention, and identify the number of divorces that occur.

Core Idea
Without intention, one revocable; intention of three valid, intention of two invalid for free woman.

This lesson covers the basic Hanafi ruling on three specific divorce utterances. The passage classifies these phrases as explicit divorce (sarih al-talaq) and explains how the husband's intention affects the number of divorces. Understanding this ruling requires knowing the default rule for sarih phrases and the linguistic capacity of the word 'talaq'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phrases
List the three utterances: 'Anti al-talaq', 'Anti taliq al-talaq', and 'Anti taliq talaqan'. Note that they vary in grammatical structure.
2
Determine the default ruling without intention
For a free woman, if the husband has no intention (or intends one or two), only one revocable divorce occurs. This is because these phrases are sarih and default to one.
3
Evaluate the effect of intended three
If the husband intends three divorces, three occur. The word 'talaq' is a generic noun (ism jins) that can imply multiplicity.
Intention of three is valid because the generic noun covers the maximum.
4
Recognize the limitation on intention of two
Intention of two is invalid because two is a specific number, and the word does not admit number. The singular adjective 'taliq' also does not admit number.
This is the majority Hanafi position, contra Imam Zufar.
5
Note the exception for slave women
If the woman is a slave, intention of two is valid because the maximum for a slave is two, and the generic noun can cover that.
This refinement shows the genus-consideration.

Source Text

ولو قال انت الطلاق أو انت طالق الطلاق أو انت طالق طلاقاً فإن لم تكن له نية أو نوى واحدة أو ثنتين فهي واحدة رجعية وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث. ووقوع الطلاق باللفظة الثانية والثالثة ظاهر لأنه لو ذكر النعت وحده يقع به الطلاق فاذا ذكره وذكر المصدر معه وانه يزيده وكادة أولى. وأما وقوعه باللفظة الأولى فان المصدر يذكر ويراد به الاسم يقال رجل عدل أي عادل فصار بمنزلة قوله انت طالق. وعلى هذا لو قال انت طلاق يقع الطلاق به أيضاً ولا يحتاج فيه إلى النية ويكون رجعياً لما بينا أنه صريح الطلاق لغلبة الاستعمال فيه. وتصح نية الثلاث لأن المصدر يحتمل العموم والكثرة لأنه اسم جنس فيعتبر لسائر أسماء الأجناس فتتناول الأدنى مع احتمال الكل. ولا تصح نية الثنتين فيها خلافاً للزفر هو يقول أن الثنتين بعض الثلث فلما صحت نية الثلث صحت نية بعضها ضرورة. ونحن نقول نية الثلث إنما صحت لكونها جنساً حتى لو كانت المرأة أمة تصح نية الثنتين باعتبار معنى الجنسية. أما الثنتان في حق الحرة عدد واللفظ لا يحتمل العدد. وهذا لأن معنى التوحد مراعى في ألفاظ الوحدان وذلك بالفردية أو الجنسية والمثنى بمعزل منهما.

ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت الطلاق یا انت طالق الطلاق یا انت طالق طلاقا ۔ پس اگر اسکی کوئی نیت نہ ہو یا ایک کی نیت کی یادو کی تو یہ ایک رجعی ہے اور اگر تین کی نیت کی تو تین ہیں اور طلاق کا واقع ہونا دوسرے اور تیسرے لفظ کے ساتھ ظاہر ہے۔ اسلئے کہ اگر یہ صرف لغت ذکر کرتا تو اس سے طلاق واقع ہو جاتی۔ پس جب اس کو ذکر کیا اور اس کے ساتھ مصدر کو ذکر کیا حال یہ کہ مصدر اس میں قوت کا اضافہ کرتا ہے تو بدرجہ اولیٰ ( واقع ہوگی ) اور بہر حال طلاق کا واقع ہونا پہلے لفظ کے ساتھ سو اسلئے کہ مصدر ذکر کیا جاتا ہے اور اس سے مرار اسم ہوتا ہے۔کہا جاتا ہے رجل عدل یعنی عادل۔ پس ( یہ ) انت طالق کے مرتبہ میں ہو گیا۔ اور اسی بنیاد پر اگر انت طلاق کہا تو اس کے ساتھ بھی طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور اس میں نیت کا محتاج نہیں ہوگا۔ اور ( طلاق رجعی ہو گی اسکی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کیا کہ یہ صریح الطلاق ہے ( طلاق کے معنی میں ) غلبہ استعمال کی وجہ سے اور تین ( طلاقوں) کی نیت کرنا صحیح ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ مصدر عموم اور کثرت کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ کیونکہ (مصدر) اسم جنس ہے۔ لہذا تمام اسماء اجناس پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔ پس ادنی کو شامل ہو گا کل کے احتمال کے ساتھ ۔ اور ان ( الفاظ مذکورہ میں ) دو ( طلاقوں ) کی نیت صحیح نہیں ہوگی۔ خلاف ہے امام زفر کا۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ دو تین کا بعض ہے۔ پس جب تین کی نیت صحیح ہے تو اس کے بعض کی نیت بالضرور صحیح ہو گی۔ اور ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نیت ثلاث اسلئے صحیح ہے کہ وہ ( کل ) جنس ہے۔ حتی کہ اگر عورت باندی ہوتی تو دو کی نیت معنی جنسیت کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے صحیح ہوتی ۔ بہر حال دو آزاد کے حق میں عدد ہے۔ اور لفظ عدد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے۔ اور یہ اسلئے کہ الفاظ وحدان میں تو حد کے معنی ملحوظ ہیں۔ اور یہ ( تو حد حاصل ہوگا ) فردیت یا جنسیت کے ساتھ ۔ اور ثنی ان دونوں سے الگ ہے۔

تشریح عبارت میں طلاق کے تین الفاظ ذکر کئے گئے ہیں۔ ان تینوں صورتوں میں اگر کوئی نیت نہیں ہے یا ایک طالق کی ہے یا دو کی تو ایک طلاق رجعی ہوگی اور اگر تین کی نسبت کی گئی ہے تو تین واقع ہو جائیں گی۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ دوسرے لفظ یعنی انـت طــالق الطلاق اور تیسرے لفظ یعنی انت طالق طلاقاً کے ساتھ طلاق کا واقع ہونا ظاہر ہے۔ کیونکہ اگر مشخص صرف صیغہ صفت (طالق) کا ذکر کرتا تو طلاق واقع ہو جاتی۔ پس جب اس نے صیغہ صفت کے ساتھ مصدر بھی ذکر کر دیا درانحالیکہ مصدر تاکید کا اضافہ کرتا ہے تو طلاق بدرجہ اولیٰ واقع ہوگی اور پہلے لفظ یعنی انت الطلاق سے طلاق کا وقوع اسلئے ہے کہ بسا اوقات مصدر بول کر اسم مراد لیا جاتا ہے جیسے رجل عدل سے رجل عادل مراد لیا گیا ہے اسی طرح یہاں الطلاق مصدر سے طالق مراد لیا جائیگا ۔ پس انت الطلاق، انت طالق کے مرتبہ میں ہو گیا اور چونکہ انت طالق سے طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ لہذا انت الطلاق سے بھی طلاق ہو جائیگی اور ایسے ہی انت طالق سے بھی طلاق واقع ہو جائیگی ۔ اس موقع پر ایک اشکال ہوگا۔ وہ یہ کہ اگر انت الطلاق انت طالق کے مرتبہ میں ہے تو اس صورت میں تین طلاقوں کی نیت صحیح نہ ہونی چاہئے ۔ جیسا کہ انت طالق میں صحیح نہیں ہے۔ جواب طالق میں تین کی نیت کرنا اس کے لغت فرد ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے۔ اور رہا لفظ الطلاق سو وہ اصل میں مصدر ہے۔ پس لفظ الطلاق من کل وجہ طالق کے مرتبہ میں نہیں ہے۔ اور انت الطالق، وقوع طلاق میں محتاج نیست بھی نہیں ہوگا۔ اور یہاں جو طالق واقع ہوگی وہ طلاق رجعی ہوگی۔ کیونکہ طلاق کے معنی میں غلبہ استعمال کی وجہ سے یہ طلاق صریحی ہے۔ اور طلاق صریحی سے طلاق رجعی واقع ہوتی ہے۔ اور محتاج نیت نہیں ہوتی ۔

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ شوہر نے اگر تین طلاقوں کی نیت کی ہے تو ان الفاظ مذکورہ میں سے ہر ایک کے ساتھ تین طلاقیں واقع ہو جائیں گی ، دلیل یہ ہے کہ تینوں الفاظ میں مصدر مذکور ہے اور مصدر عموم اور کثرت کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ کیونکہ مصدر اسم جنس ہے۔ لہذا اس کو
And if he says 'Anti al-talaq' or 'Anti taliq al-talaq' or 'Anti taliq talaqan', then if he has no intention, or intends one or two, it is one revocable divorce. If he intends three, then three. The occurrence of divorce by the second and third phrases is apparent, because if he mentioned the adjective alone, divorce occurs by it. So if he mentions the adjective and with it the verbal noun, which adds strength, then it is even more so. As for the occurrence by the first phrase, it is because the verbal noun is mentioned and what is meant is the noun: it is said 'rajul 'adl' i.e. 'adil'. So it becomes like his saying 'Anti taliq'. Similarly, if he says 'Anti talaq', divorce also occurs by it without need for intention, and it is revocable because it is explicit divorce due to common usage. Intention of three is valid because the verbal noun can imply generality and multiplicity, as it is a generic noun, so analogized to all generic nouns, covering the minimum while allowing the maximum. Intention of two is not valid, contrary to Imam Zufar who says two is part of three, so if intention of three is valid, intention of part is necessarily valid. We reply: intention of three is valid because it is a genus; if the woman were a slave, intention of two would be valid by considering the meaning of genus. But two for a free woman is a number, and the word does not admit number. This is because the meaning of singleness is observed in singular words, either by individuality or genus, and dual is separate from both.

Translation: And if he said 'Anti al-talaq' or 'Anti taliq al-talaq' or 'Anti taliq talaqan', then if he has no intention, or intended one or two, it is one revocable; if he intended three, then three. The occurrence of divorce with the second and third phrases is apparent because if he had only mentioned the adjective, divorce would have occurred. So when he mentioned it and with it the verbal noun, while the verbal noun adds strength, then it is even more so. As for the occurrence with the first phrase, it is because the verbal noun is mentioned and what is meant is the noun: it is said 'rajul 'adl' meaning just. So it becomes in the place of 'Anti taliq'. On this basis, if he said 'Anti talaq', divorce also occurs with it without need for intention, and it is revocable because it is explicit divorce due to common usage. Intention of three is valid because the verbal noun can imply generality and multiplicity, as it is a generic noun, so analogized to all generic nouns, covering the minimum while allowing the maximum. And intention of two is not valid in these phrases, contrary to Imam Zufar. He says that two is part of three, so if intention of three is valid, intention of its part is necessarily valid. We reply: intention of three is valid only because it is a genus; if the woman were a slave, intention of two would be valid by considering the meaning of genus. But for a free woman, two is a number, and the word does not admit number. This is because in singular words the meaning of unity is observed, either by individuality or genus, and dual is separate from both.

Explanation: In the text, three phrases of divorce are mentioned. In all three cases, if there is no intention, or intention of one or two, then one revocable divorce occurs. If intention of three, then three occur. The author of Hidayah says: The occurrence of divorce with the second phrase (Anti taliq al-talaq) and third phrase (Anti taliq talaqan) is apparent, because if the speaker had only mentioned the adjective (taliq), divorce would have occurred. So when he mentioned the adjective and also the verbal noun, while the verbal noun adds emphasis, then divorce occurs even more so. As for the occurrence with the first phrase (Anti al-talaq), it is because sometimes the verbal noun is uttered and what is meant is the noun, like 'rajul 'adl' meaning a just man. So here 'al-talaq' (verbal noun) is taken to mean 'taliq', so 'Anti al-talaq' becomes in the place of 'Anti taliq'. And since divorce occurs with 'Anti taliq', it also occurs with 'Anti al-talaq'. Similarly, 'Anti taliq' also results in divorce. At this point, an objection arises: if 'Anti al-talaq' is like 'Anti taliq', then intention of three should not be valid, just as it is not valid in 'Anti taliq'. The answer is: In 'Anti taliq', intention of three is not valid because it is a singular adjective. As for the word 'al-talaq', it is originally a verbal noun. So 'al-talaq' is not entirely like 'taliq'. And 'Anti al-talaq' does not need intention for the occurrence of divorce. And the divorce that occurs here is revocable, because due to common usage it is explicit divorce, and explicit divorce effects a revocable divorce without need for intention.

The author of Hidayah says: If the husband intended three divorces, then with each of the mentioned phrases three divorces occur. The proof is that all three phrases contain the verbal noun, and the verbal noun can imply generality and multiplicity, because it is a generic noun. So it is analogized.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on three particular phrases of divorce: 'Anti al-talaq' (you are the divorce), 'Anti taliq al-talaq' (you are divorced, the divorce), and 'Anti taliq talaqan' (you are divorced, a divorce). The ruling is that if the husband has no intention, or intends one or two divorces, only one revocable divorce occurs. If he intends three, then three occur. The text then explains the reasoning. The occurrence of divorce with the second and third phrases is straightforward because they explicitly mention both the adjective (taliq) and the verbal noun (talaq), which strengthens the divorce. The first phrase 'Anti al-talaq' is treated as if he said 'Anti taliq' because the verbal noun is sometimes used to mean the noun (e.g., 'rajul 'adl' meaning a just man). Thus it is also clear divorce without need for intention. The text then addresses the validity of intention: intention of three is valid because the verbal noun (talaq) as a generic noun can imply generality and multiplicity. Intention of two is not valid for a free woman, because two is a specific number and the word does not admit number. This is contrary to Imam Zufar, who argued that since two is part of three, and intention of three is valid, intention of two should also be valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes that three is a genus, while two is a number; the word only admits genus, not specific numbers. However, if the woman were a slave, intention of two would be valid because the maximum for a slave is two, so the word's generality can cover that. The passage concludes that all three phrases are explicit (sarih) and effect a revocable divorce without need for intention, but intention can affect the number if it is possible (three for a free woman, two for a slave).

Key Terms
صريح الطلاق (sarih al-talaq): Explicit divorce phrases that clearly indicate divorce without need for intention, such as 'Anti taliq' or 'Anti al-talaq'.
نعت فرد (na't fard): A singular adjective that describes one individual; it does not admit number (dual or plural).
اسم جنس (ism jins): A generic noun that denotes a category or genus; it can imply both a single instance and the whole category, but not a specific intermediate number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his free wife 'Anti taliq al-talaq' with no intention. How many divorces occur?
A) Zero, because intention is required.
B) One revocable divorce.
C) Three divorces because the phrase includes both adjective and verbal noun.
D) One irrevocable divorce.

Key Takeaway
For these three phrases, without intention or with intention of one or two, one revocable divorce occurs; only intention of three is recognized for a free woman.
