Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Intention with Masdar-Based Talāq Phrases: Imam Zufar's Dispute
Explains the effect of intention when uttering three specific talāq phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intending two divorces when using masdar-based talāq phrases and explain the opposing view of Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
The masdar as ism jins only permits intention for one or the full genus, not an intermediate number.

This passage addresses a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school. When a husband uses phrases like 'anti al-talāq', the masdar (infinitive) behaves as a generic noun (ism jins), limiting valid intentions to either the minimum (one) or the entire genus (three for a free woman). Imam Zufar argues that two should also be valid since it is part of three. We will dissect the linguistic reasoning and the majority response that settles the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Phrases and Default Ruling
The passage mentions three phrases: 'anti al-talāq', 'anti tāliq al-talāq', and 'anti tāliq talāqan'. First, note that without intention or with intention for one, a single rajʿī divorce occurs; with intention for three, three occur. This is the default ruling.
The first phrase is unique because it uses the masdar alone.
2
Understand the Role of the Masdar as Ism Jins
The masdar here is treated as a generic noun (ism jins). Like other generic nouns (e.g., 'rajulun ʿadl' meaning 'just man'), it can indicate either one individual or the entire genus. For a free woman, the genus of talāq is three (the maximum permissible). Thus, the masdar can indicate one or three.
This linguistic principle is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze Why Intending Two Is Invalid
Intending two divorces for a free woman is invalid because two is neither the minimum individual (one) nor the entire genus (three). It is a numerical intermediate that the generic noun cannot express. The masdar only admits extremes: one or the whole.
Contrast with a slave woman, for whom the genus is two, making intention for two valid.
4
Present Imam Zufar's Argument
Imam Zufar argues that two is part of three. Since intending three (the whole) is valid, intending its part (two) should also be valid logically. He uses a part-whole analogy.
This is a qiyas jaliy based on the relationship between two and three.
5
Examine the Hanafi Response and Tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) respond that two is not a 'part' of the genus in the same sense; it is a number that the masdar cannot accommodate. The masdar's capacity is limited to the minimum and the full genus. For a free woman, the full genus is three, so only one and three are valid. This position is the mufta bihi.
Imam Zufar's view is acknowledged as a valid ikhtilaf but not followed in fatwa.

Translation: And if he said 'anti al-talāq' or 'anti tāliq al-talāq' or 'anti tāliq talāqan'. Then if he had no intention or intended one or two, then it is one rajʿī divorce, and if he intended three, then they are three. The occurrence of divorce with the second and third word is obvious. Because if he only mentioned the word itself, divorce would occur. So when he mentioned it with the infinitive, and the infinitive adds strength, then it is even more likely. And in any case, the occurrence of divorce with the first word is because the infinitive is sometimes used to mean the noun. It is said 'rajulun ʿadl' meaning ʿādil. So it became at the level of 'anti tāliq'. And on this basis, if he said 'anti talāq', divorce would occur with it as well. And it does not need intention. And it will be a rajʿī divorce because of what we have stated that it is ṣarīḥ al-talāq due to preponderance of usage. And intending three divorces is valid because the infinitive is general and connotes multiplicity. Because it is an ism jins, so it is analogized to all ism jins. So it includes the minimum with the possibility of the whole. And in these words, intending two divorces is not valid, contrary to Imam Zufar. He says that two is part of three. So when intending three is valid, intending its part is necessarily valid. We answer that intending three is valid because it is the whole genus. Even if the woman were a slave, then intending two would be valid considering the meaning of genus. However, two for a free woman is a number, and the word does not admit number. This is because words of unity consider the meaning of limit, and that limit is achieved with individuality or with genus. Two is separate from both.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of intention when a husband utters one of three specific phrases: 'anti al-talāq', 'anti tāliq al-talāq', or 'anti tāliq talāqan'. The ruling: if the husband has no intention or intends one or two divorces, only one rajʿī divorce occurs; if he intends three, then three divorces occur. The commentator explains that the occurrence of divorce with the second and third phrases is obvious because the infinitive (masdar) emphasizes the action. The first phrase 'anti al-talāq' is treated like 'anti tāliq' because the infinitive is sometimes used in place of the active participle. The divorce that occurs is rajʿī because these phrases are considered ṣarīḥ (explicit) due to common usage. The text then addresses why intending two divorces is invalid: the masdar is an ism jins (generic noun) that can only indicate one (the minimum) or the totality (three for a free woman, two for a slave). Two is a number that does not fit this pattern. Imam Zufar disagrees, arguing that two is part of three and should be valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes between a part (juz) and a number; the masdar cannot denote a number like two for a free woman. The commentary further clarifies that if the woman is a slave, intending two is valid because two is the maximum for a slave and thus represents the whole genus.

Key Terms
مصدر (masdar): The verbal noun; in this context, it is used as a generic noun (ism jins) that can indicate the minimum occurrence or the entire genus.
اسم جنس (ism jins): A generic noun that denotes the genus without specifying number; it can indicate one or the whole kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a free woman's husband says 'anti al-talāq' and intends two divorces, what is the ruling?
A) Two divorces occur.
B) One rajʿī divorce occurs.
C) Three divorces occur.
D) No divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
When using masdar-based talāq phrases, only one or the full count (three for free, two for slave) can be intended; intermediate numbers are invalid.

Talaq — Divorce

Intentions for Two Divorces When Saying 'Anti Tāliq al-Talāq'
Ruling when husband intends separate divorces from each part of the phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'anti tāliq al-talāq' with separate intentions and explain the reasoning behind accepting his claim.

Core Idea
Each word in 'tāliq al-talāq' can effect a divorce, so two rajʿī divorces occur if consummated; else one bāʾin.

When a husband uses an ambiguous divorce phrase like 'anti tāliq al-talāq', the law must determine how many divorces occur. If he later clarifies that he intended separate divorces from each word, the Hanafi school accepts his claim because each word is independently capable of effecting divorce. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the husband’s claim
The husband says 'anti tāliq al-talāq' and later claims he meant one divorce from 'tāliq' and one from 'al-talāq'.
The phrase is ambiguous, so the husband's clarification matters.
2
Check if each word is capable of effecting divorce
Both 'tāliq' and 'al-talāq' are valid words for pronouncing divorce in Hanafi fiqh.
This is the basis for the ruling.
3
Apply the rule of tasdīq (belief in his claim)
Because the words are ambiguous and he is in a position to know his intent, his claim is believed.
This is a general Hanafi principle for ambiguous expressions.
4
Distinguish between consummated and unconsummated wife
If she is consummated (madkhūl bihā), two rajʿī divorces occur. If unconsummated, the first word makes her bāʾin and the second is superfluous.
The difference arises because a non-consummated wife becomes bāʾin after one divorce, so the second cannot take effect.
5
Conclude the number and type of divorces
For a consummated wife: two rajʿī divorces. For an unconsummated wife: one bāʾin divorce.
This matches treating the phrase as 'anti tāliq wa tāliq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال انت طالق الطلاق وقال اردت بقولى طالق واحدة وبقولى الطلاق أخرى يصدق لان كل واحد منها صالح للايقاع فـكـانـه قال انت طالق وطالق فتقع رجعيتان اذا كـانـت مـدخـول بـهــا ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے کہا انت طالق الطلاق اور کہا کہ میں نے اپنے قول طالق سے ایک طلاق کا ارادہ کیا ہے اور اپنے قول الطلاق سے دوسری کا تو تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک (لفظ طلاق) واقع کرنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے۔ پس گویا اس نے کہا انت طالق و طالق ۔ پس دور جعی واقع ہوں گی جبکہ وہ (عورت) مدخول بہا ہو۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ ظاہر ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ طالق اور طلاق دونوں میں سے ہر ایک لفظ طلاق واقع کرنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے۔ پس اگر وہ عورت غیر مدخول بہا ہے تو پہلے لفظ طالق سے بائنہ ہو جائے گی۔ اور دوسرا لفظ لغو ہوگا۔ اور اگر مدخول بہا ہے تو دوطلاق رجعی واقع ہوں گی اور ایک لفظ طالق سے اور دوسری لفظ الطلاق سے تقدیر مبتداء تقدیری عبارت ہوگی انت طالق انت الطلاق جیسے انت طالق و طالق کی تقدیری عبارت ہے۔ انت طالق وانت طالق ۔
And if he said 'anti tāliq al-talāq' and said: 'I intended by my word tāliq one divorce and by my word al-talāq another', he is believed because each of them is capable of effecting divorce. So it is as if he said 'anti tāliq wa tāliq', and two rajʿī divorces occur if she is a consummated wife. Commentary: The case is clear. The proof is that each of the words tāliq and talāq is capable of effecting divorce. So if she is an unconsummated wife, the first word tāliq makes her bāʾin, and the second word is superfluous. If she is consummated, two rajʿī divorces occur: one from the word tāliq and the second from the word al-talāq, with the implied predicate being 'anti tāliq anti al-talāq' just as 'anti tāliq wa tāliq' is implied to be 'anti tāliq wa anti tāliq'.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when a husband says 'anti tāliq al-talāq' and then explains that he intended one divorce from 'tāliq' and another from 'al-talāq'. The Hanafi school accepts his claim (he is believed) because each word is independently capable of effecting a divorce. The effect: if the wife has been consummated, two rajʿī divorces occur; if not consummated, the first word makes her bāʾin (irrevocably divorced) and the second becomes irrelevant. The reasoning is that the phrase is treated as though he said 'anti tāliq wa tāliq' (you are divorced and divorced). The commentary reinforces that both 'tāliq' and 'al-talāq' are valid words for pronouncing divorce.

Key Terms
تصدیق (tasdīq): The husband is believed (his claim of intention is accepted) because the words are ambiguous and he is in a position to know his intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'anti tāliq al-talāq' to his consummated wife and intends one divorce from 'tāliq' and one from 'al-talāq', what is the result?
A) One bāʾin divorce.
B) One rajʿī divorce.
C) Two rajʿī divorces.
D) Three divorces.

Key Takeaway
When a husband uses an ambiguous divorce phrase and later clarifies separate intentions, each valid word effects its own divorce, subject to the wife's consummation status.

Divorce by Attributing to a Part Representing the Whole
Ruling when divorce is attributed to the whole woman or a part representing the whole

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when divorce is attributed to the wife's entire body or a part that conventionally represents the whole, and cite the textual evidence.

Core Idea
A part that conventionally represents the whole, like the neck or face, suffices to effect divorce.

In Arabic usage, certain body parts are commonly used to refer to the entire person. The Hanafi school applies this linguistic convention to divorce: if a husband says 'your neck is divorced' or similar, it is treated as if he said 'you are divorced' because the part stands for the whole. This lesson examines the ruling and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Expression
Look at what part the husband attributed the divorce to. Determine if it is the whole woman (e.g., 'you are divorced') or a part of her body.
The passage covers both explicit reference to the whole and parts that represent the whole.
2
Check Convention
Evaluate whether the part mentioned is conventionally used in Arabic to stand for the entire person, such as neck (raqabah), face (wajh), head (ra's), body (jasad/badan), private part (farj), etc.
The text provides examples: neck, face, head, body, private part, blood (in some contexts).
3
Apply the Ruling
If the expression uses a part that conventionally represents the whole, divorce occurs immediately, without need for intention (niyyah). The husband's words are treated as clear (sarih) in divorce.
This is because the part stands in for the whole in common parlance.
4
Cite the Evidence
Recall the Quranic verses and Hadith cited: 'tahrīr raqabah' (freeing a neck = freeing a slave), 'facallat aʿnāquhum' (their necks remained bowed), and the hadith about private parts. These show that parts are used to refer to the whole.
The dalālah (indication) is zanni but strong due to widespread usage.
5
Differentiate Non-representative Parts
If the part does not conventionally represent the whole (e.g., hand, foot), divorce does not occur unless the husband intended divorce. This passage does not cover those cases, but it is an important distinction.
The key is 'mā yuʿabbaru bihi ʿan al-jumlah' – what expresses the whole.

Source Text

And if he attributes divorce to her entirety or to that which expresses the entirety, divorce occurs because he attributed it to its locus. Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that if a husband attributes divorce to the entire woman or to a part that conventionally represents the whole, divorce occurs. The reason is that the divorce is attributed to its proper place (the wife). Examples include saying 'your neck is divorced' or 'your face is divorced', etc. The commentator supports this with textual evidence: the Quran uses 'neck' to refer to the whole slave (tahrīr raqabah), and the Hadith uses 'private parts' to refer to the whole person. Arabic usage also treats 'head' and 'face' as representatives of the entire person. Thus, these expressions effect divorce immediately without need for intention, as they are clear in referring to the wife.

Key Terms
اضاف الطلاق (aḍāfa al-ṭalāq): He attributed divorce; i.e., he linked the word divorce to a part of the woman or her entirety.

Intention of Two Divorces with 'Al-Talaq': The Hanafi Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of 'al-talaq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school restricts the word 'al-talaq' to one or the total (three for a free woman, two for a slave-girl) and apply this principle to determine the validity of intended numbers.

Core Idea
When 'al-talaq' is uttered, only the real individual (one) or the legal individual (total) is valid—never an intermediate number like two.

The word 'al-talaq' (the divorce) is a noun of genus in Hanafi usul. This means it can denote either the minimal instance (one divorce) or the entire class (the maximum number, e.g., three for a free woman). But what if a husband intends exactly two divorces? The majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say it is not valid, while Imam Zufar argues it should be. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on intending two divorces with 'al-talaq'
Read the passage and identify the ruling: using 'al-talaq' with the intention of two divorces is not valid according to the majority. Only one (real individual) or three (legal individual for a free woman) are possible.
The ruling applies specifically to a free husband. For a slave-girl, two is the total, so it is valid.
2
Identify the opponent and their argument
Imam Zufar argues by analogy: since three (the total) is valid, its part (two) should also be valid.
He treats two as a share of the total genus.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (the proof)
The Hanafi response is linguistic: 'al-talaq' is a noun of genus (ism jins) which has only two categories: the real individual (fard haqiqi) = one divorce, and the legal individual (fard hukmi) = the total. Two is neither; it is a mere number, not a true individual.
The masdar (verbal noun) is among words of unity (alfaz al-wahdan); it respects the limit, which is realized only with the minimum or the maximum.
4
Apply the principle to the slave-girl exception
For a slave-girl, the total (legal individual) is two divorces. Therefore, intending two with 'al-talaq' is valid because it coincides with the legal individual.
This consistency confirms the rule: the word can only denote one or the total.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a free husband says 'al-talaq' intending two, only one divorce occurs. If a slave-owner says it for his slave-girl intending two, two divorces occur. This difference matters for further rulings like iddah, remarriage, etc.
The disagreement is real: Imam Zufar would rule two divorces even for the free man, requiring different legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمام اسماء اجناس پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔ پس مصدر ادنی (ایک) کو شامل ہو گا کل کے احتمال کے ساتھ ۔ البتہ ان الفاظ میں اگر دو طلاقوں کی نیت کی ہے تو ہمارے نزدیک صحیح نہیں ۔ اور امام زفر فر ماتے ہیں کہ دو کی نیت بھی صحیح ہے امام زفر دلیل میں فرماتے ہیں کہ دو طلاقیں تین کا جز ہیں۔ پس جب تین ( کل ) کی نیت درست ہے تو اسکے جزا اور بعض کی نیت بھی درست ہوگی۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مصدر اسم جنس ہے اور اہم جنس کے دو فرد ہوتے ہیں ایک فرد حقیقی دوم فرد کمی ۔ فرد حقیقی ایک طلاق ہے اور فرد حکمی کل کا مجموعہ یعنی تین طلاقیں ہیں اور دو کا عدد طلاق کا نہ فرد حقیقی ہے اور نہ فرد دکھی ۔ پس اگر شوہر نے تین طلاقوں کی نیت کی ہے تو یہ مصدر کا فرد کمی ہونے کی وجہ سے صحیح ہے۔ چنانچہ عورت اگر باندی ہے تو اسکے حق میں دو طلاقوں کی نیت کرنا بھی صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ دو طلاق باندی کے حق میں کل جنس یعنی فرد کی ہے۔ اور آزاد عورت کے حق میں دو طلاق عدد محض ہے اور مصدر عدد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے کیونکہ مصدر الفاظ وحدان میں سے ہے۔ اور الفاظ وحدان میں تو حد کے معنی کی رعایت کی جاتی ہے۔ اور تو حد کے معنی فرد حقیقی کے ساتھ تحقق ہوں گے یا فرد کمی یعنی کل جنس کے ساتھ ۔ اور دو کا عددان دونوں سے الگ ہے کیونکہ وہ نہ فرد حقیقی ہے اور نہ فرد کمی ۔
It will be measured on all nouns of genus. So the verbal noun (masdar) includes the minimal (one) while also implying the total. However, if in these words he intended two divorces, it is not valid according to us. Imam Zufar says that the intention of two is also valid. Imam Zufar argues that two divorces are part of three. So when the intention of three (the total) is valid, then the intention of its part (two) is also valid. Our proof is that the verbal noun is a noun of genus, and for a genus there are two individuals: one real individual and one legal individual. The real individual is one divorce, and the legal individual is the total (three divorces). And the number two is neither a real individual nor a legal individual. So if the husband intended three divorces, it is valid because it is the legal individual of the masdar. Just as if the woman is a slave-girl, then the intention of two divorces is also valid for her, because two divorces are the total genus for a slave-girl. And for a free woman, two is a mere number, and the masdar does not imply number, because the masdar is among words of unity. And in words of unity, consideration is given to the meaning of limit. And limit is realized with the real individual or with the legal individual (the total genus). And the number two is separate from both, because it is neither a real individual nor a legal individual.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the word 'al-talaq' (the divorce) when the husband intends two divorces. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and the majority) is that 'al-talaq' is a noun of genus (ism jins). As such, it can signify either one divorce (the real individual, fard haqiqi) or the entire class of divorce, which for a free woman is three (the legal individual, fard hukmi). It does not signify the number two because two is neither the real individual nor the total class. Therefore, if a husband says 'al-talaq' intending two, it is not valid; only one or three are possible. Imam Zufar argues by analogy: since three (the total) is valid, its part (two) should also be valid. The Hanafi response rejects this because the verbal noun's capacity is limited to the two categories. An exception is made for a slave-girl, for whom two divorces constitute the total (since a slave-girl can only be divorced twice). In that case, intending two is valid because it is the legal individual for her.

Key Terms
مصدر (masdar): The verbal noun, here specifically the word 'al-talaq' used as a formula for divorce
اسم جنس (ism jins): A noun that denotes a genus or category, not a specific number
فرد حقیقی (fard haqiqi): The real individual, here one divorce
فرد حکمی (fard hukmi): The legal individual, here the entire class of divorce (three for a free woman, two for a slave-girl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a free man says 'al-talaq' intending two divorces, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Two divorces occur
B) Three divorces occur
C) One divorce occurs
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
The word 'al-talaq' can only produce one or the total number of divorces (three for a free woman, two for a slave-girl); any intermediate intention is not realized.

Ruling on Saying 'Anti Taliq al-Talaq'
Ruling on double phrasing with 'taliq' and 'al-talaq' consecutively

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq' and differentiate between consummated and non-consummated cases.

Core Idea
Each word in 'anti taliq al-talaq' can effect a separate divorce, with outcome depending on consummation.

This lesson examines a specific divorce formula where two potential divorce words are used consecutively. The husband may clarify his intent, and the Hanafi school recognizes both words as valid. The key factor is whether the marriage was consummated, as this determines if one or two divorces occur and whether they are revocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and intent
The husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq'. He may later clarify that he intended each word as a separate divorce. Note his intent is accepted as per Hanafi ruling.
The clarification is part of the scenario.
2
Recognize each word as a valid divorce formula
Both 'taliq' and 'al-talaq' are capable of effecting divorce. Hanafis accept 'taliq' as sarih (clear) and 'al-talaq' as kinayah (allusive) but effective with intent.
This is the core proof: each word is capable.
3
Determine the wife's status
Check if the wife is madhkhul biha (consummated) or non-consummated. This changes the legal outcome.
Consummation is a condition for multiple revocable divorces.
4
Apply the ruling for consummated wife
If she is consummated, two revocable divorces (talaq raj'i) occur: one from 'taliq' and one from 'al-talaq'.
Each word is independent.
5
Apply the ruling for non-consummated wife
If she is non-consummated, the first word 'taliq' effects one irrevocable divorce (talaq bain). The second word 'al-talaq' is wasted because the marriage has already ended.
A non-consummated wife can only be given one divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if one says: 'You are divorced, the divorce' and says: 'I intended by my saying 'taliq' one divorce, and by my saying 'al-talaq' another', he is believed, because each of them is capable of effecting divorce. So it is as if he said: 'You are divorced and divorced'. So two revocable divorces occur if she is consummated. Translation: And if someone said 'anti taliq al-talaq' and said that he intended one divorce by the word 'taliq' and another by the word 'al-talaq', he is believed, because each of these words is capable of effecting divorce. So it is as if he said 'anti taliq wa taliq'. So two revocable divorces occur if the woman is consummated. Explanation: The case is clear. The proof is that both words 'taliq' and 'talaq' are each capable of effecting divorce. If the woman is non-consummated, then the first word 'taliq' makes her bain (irrevocable), and the second word is redundant. And if she is consummated, two revocable divorces occur, one from the word 'taliq' and the second from the word 'al-talaq', by way of implied predicate, the implied phrase being 'anti taliq anti al-talaq', just as the implied phrase for 'anti taliq wa taliq' is 'anti taliq wa anti taliq'.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq' (you are divorced, the divorce) and clarifies that he intended each expression as a separate divorce, he is believed. Both 'taliq' and 'al-talaq' are valid words to effect divorce. The consequence depends on whether the woman is consummated (madhkhul biha) or not. If she is non-consummated, the first word 'taliq' makes her bain (irrevocably divorced), and the second word is wasted. If she is consummated, two revocable divorces occur because each word is independent. The construction is interpreted as two separate sentences: 'You are divorced, you are the divorce.' This is similar to saying 'anti taliq wa taliq'. The Sababayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree with this ruling.

Key Terms
طالق (taliq): An active participle meaning 'divorced', used as a formula to effect divorce
الطلاق (al-talaq): The verbal noun 'the divorce', also used as a formula
مدخول بہا (madhkhul biha): A woman with whom the husband has consummated the marriage

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'anti taliq al-talaq', each word can effect a divorce, and the number depends on consummation.

Divorce by Referring to a Part Denoting the Whole Woman
Ruling on divorcing by referring to a part signifying the whole woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce when the husband uses a part that signifies the whole woman, identify the proofs, and apply the rule to various expressions.

Core Idea
If a husband says 'your neck is divorced', divorce occurs because 'neck' is a synecdoche for the whole person.

Divorce can be expressed in many ways, not only by the direct phrase 'anti taliq'. The Hanafi school holds that if a husband attributes divorce to a part of his wife's body that is conventionally used to signify the whole person, the divorce is effective. This lesson explores the ruling, the rationale, and the linguistic proofs behind it, including Quranic verses and prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The author states that if divorce is added to the whole woman or to a part that denotes the whole, divorce occurs. First, note the examples: 'anti taliq' for the whole, and terms like 'neck', 'head', 'body', 'private part', etc. for parts.
2
Locate the linguistic principle
The principle is that these parts are synecdoches—they stand for the entire body. The author explains that 'jasad' and 'badan' clearly mean the whole body, and similar usage extends to other terms.
The key is 'ma yu'abbiru bihi 'ani al-jumla' (that which is used to express the whole).
3
Examine the proofs (dalil)
The text provides three types of proof: Quranic verses (fal-tahriru raqaba', the neck meaning a slave), a hadith (curse on 'furooj' meaning women), and common Arabic expressions ('ra's al-qawm' meaning chief).
Analyze wajh al-dalala: how each proof shows that a part can denote the whole.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a difference of opinion regarding the word 'dam' (blood). One narration says divorce occurs, another says no. The Hidayah prefers the view that it does occur.
This shows that not all terms are universally accepted; the school has a preferred position.
5
Apply the ruling to new expressions
Practice: given a statement like 'your blood is divorced', apply the mufta bihi ruling. Also consider terms like 'your face' or 'your hand'—which parts are included? The text includes 'face' because it is used to denote the whole person (e.g., 'wajh al-arab').
Some parts like 'hand' (yad) may not be included unless commonly used to signify the whole; the text does not mention it.

یا تیرا بدن یا تیرا جسد یا تیری شرمگاہ یا تیرا چہرہ۔ کیونکہ ان الفاظ کے ذریعہ تمام بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ بہر حال جسد اور بدن تو ظاہر ہے۔ اور ایسے ہی ان دونوں کے علاوہ ۔ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا فتحـريـر رقبة یعنی آزاد کرنا ایک گردن اور فرمایا فظلت اعناقهم یعنی خاضعین وہ لوگ ہیں جو خضوع کرنے والے ہیں ۔ اور آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا لعنت کرے اللہ شرمگاہوں پر جو زینوں پر ہوں۔ اور کہا جاتا ہے فلاں شخص راس القوم ہے۔ اور وجہ العرب اور اس کی ذات ہلاک ہو گئی ۔ اور اسی قبیل سے ایک روایت میں دم ہے۔ کہا جاتا ہے اس کا خون رائیگاں ہے اور اسی قبیل سے ( لفظ ) نفس ہے۔ اور وہ ظاہر ہے۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری نے ایک ضابطہ بیان کیا ہے وہ یہ کہ اگر طلاق کل عورت کی طرف منسوب کی گئی یا ایسے جز کی طرف جس سے کل عورت کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں طلاق اپنے محل یعنی عورت کی طرف منسوب کی گئی ہے۔ کل عورت کی طرف منسوب کرنے کی مثال انت طالق ہے۔ کیونکہ تا عورت کی ضمیر ہے جس سے کل عورت مراد ہو گی اور جز کی طرف منسوب کرنے کی مثالیں رقبتک طالق الی آخرہ ہیں۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ان مذکورہ الفاظ سے جمیع بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ بدن اور جسد سے جمیع بدن کا تعبیر ہونا تو ظاہر ہے۔ اور ایسے ہی ان دونوں کے علاوہ دوسرے مذکورہ الفاظ سے بھی جمیع بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ مثلاً کلام پاک میں ہے فتحرير رقبة آیت میں نفس رقبہ مراد نہیں بلکہ ذات مراد ہے۔ اور فرمایا فظلت اعناقهم آیت میں نفس اعناق مراد نہیں بلکہ ذوات اور اشخاص مراد ہیں۔ کیونکہ اگر نفس اعناق مراد ہوتا تو لہا خاضعین کے بجائے لہا خاضعۂ ہوتا۔ اور حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا لعن الله الفروج على السروج - اس حدیث میں نفس فروج مراد نہیں۔ بلکہ صاحبہ فروج یعنی عورتوں کی ذات مراد ہے۔ کیونکہ لعنت فرج پر نہیں ہوتی بلکہ ذات پر ہوتی ہے۔ یہ حدیث غریب ہے۔ البتہ اس کے ہم معنی ایک دوسری حدیث ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذوات الفرج ان يركبن السروج اور کہا جاتا ہے فلان رأس القوم یعنی اکبر هم اور وجہ العرب یعنی او جھھم ۔ ان دونوں مقولوں میں نفس راس اور نفس وجہ مراد نہیں ۔ بلکہ ذات اور شخص مراد ہے۔ اور ان دونوں مقولوں سے زیادہ واضح استدلال یہ ہے کہ دعا کے موقع پر کہا جاتا ہے یعیش راسک ظاہر ہے کہ زندہ رہنے کی دعا کسی ایک جز کیلئے نہیں ہوتی ۔ بلکہ پوری ذات کیلئے ہوتی ہے اور وجہ سے ذات میں روح سے نفس روح مراد نہیں بلکہ پوری ذات مراد ہے۔ اور ایک روایت میں دم بھی اسی قبیل سے ہے۔ یعنی دم سے پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے، کہا جاتا ہے دمہ هدر۔ لہذا اگر دم کی طرف طلاق منسوب کی جائے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور ایک روایت یہ ہے کہ دم کی طرف طلاق منسوب کرنے سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ اور لفظ نفس بھی اسی قبیل سے ہے کہ اس کی طرف طلاق منسوب کرنے سے طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ یہ ظاہر ہے کیونکہ نفس سے مراد ہی ذات ہے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
And when he adds divorce to her whole (jamlataha) or to that which is used to express the whole, divorce occurs, because it has been added to its place. That is like when he says 'anti taliq' because the 'ta' is the pronoun for the woman, or says 'your neck is divorced' or 'your right is divorced' or 'your head is divorced' or 'your spirit is divorced' or 'your body' or 'your physical form' or 'your private part' or 'your face' - because these words are used to express the entire body. As for 'jasad' and 'badan', it is obvious, and likewise other than these. Allah Almighty said: 'So the freeing of a neck' and said: 'So their necks are bent' and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'May Allah curse the private parts upon the saddles.' And it is said 'So-and-so is the head of the people' and 'the face of the Arabs' and 'his spirit perished' meaning himself. And from this category is 'blood' in one narration: it is said 'his blood is wasted'. And from it is 'nafs' (self), and it is obvious. Translation: And when a man adds divorce to the whole woman or to a part by which the whole is expressed, divorce occurs, because divorce is added to its place. This is like saying 'anti taliq' because the 'ta' is the pronoun for the woman, or saying 'your neck is divorced' or 'your right is divorced' or 'your head is divorced' or 'your spirit' or 'your body' or 'your physical form' or 'your private part' or 'your face' - because by these words the entire body is expressed. As for 'jasad' and 'badan', it is obvious, and likewise other than these. Allah Almighty said: 'So the freeing of a neck' and said: 'So their necks are bent' and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'May Allah curse the private parts upon the saddles.' And it is said 'So-and-so is the head of the people' and 'the face of the Arabs' and 'his spirit perished' meaning himself. And from this category is 'blood' in one narration: it is said 'his blood is wasted'. And from it is 'nafs' (self), and it is obvious. Explanation: The author of al-Quduri stated a principle: if divorce is added to the whole woman or to a part that expresses the whole woman, divorce occurs, because in that case divorce is added to its place (the woman). An example of adding to the whole is 'anti taliq' because the 'ta' is the pronoun for the woman, referring to the whole. Examples of adding to a part are 'raqabatuki taliq' etc. The author of al-Hidayah says that from these mentioned words the entire body is expressed. That 'badan' and 'jasad' express the entire body is obvious, and likewise other mentioned words. For example, in the holy verse 'fa tahriru raqaba' (so freeing of a neck), the individual neck is not meant, but the person is meant. And in 'fa zallat a'naquhum' (so their necks are bent), the individual necks are not meant, but the persons are meant, because if the necks themselves were meant, it would have been 'lakha khadi'atan' instead of 'lakha khadi'een'. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'May Allah curse the private parts upon the saddles' - in this hadith, the private parts themselves are not meant, but the owners of the private parts (the women) are meant, because the curse is not on the private part but on the person. This hadith is ghareeb. However, there is another hadith with the same meaning from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet forbade women to ride saddles. And it is said 'fulan ra's al-qawm' (so-and-so is the head of the people) meaning their chief, and 'wajh al-arab' (the face of the Arabs) meaning their leader. In these two sayings, the individual head and face are not meant, but the person is meant. And clearer than these two sayings is that in supplication it is said 'ya'ishu ra'suka' (may your head live) - obviously the supplication is not for a single part but for the whole self. And 'wajh' is used for the self, and 'ruh' for the self, not the spirit alone. And in one narration, 'dam' (blood) is also from this category, meaning that the whole body is expressed by blood. It is said 'damuhu hadar' (his blood is wasted). Therefore, if divorce is added to 'dam', divorce occurs. And there is another narration that divorce does not occur by adding to 'dam'. And the word 'nafs' is also from this category - adding divorce to it effects divorce. This is obvious because 'nafs' means the self. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its proofs: if a husband attributes divorce to the whole woman or to a part that is commonly used to signify the whole woman, divorce occurs. The author cites examples like 'neck', 'head', 'body', 'private part', etc. The reason is that such usage is metaphorical but well-established in Arabic. The text provides several proofs: Quranic verses using 'raqaba' (neck) to mean the whole person, and 'a'naq' (necks) to mean people; a hadith where 'furooj' (private parts) is used to refer to women; common expressions like 'ra's al-qawm' (head of the people) meaning the chief; and the supplication 'ya'ishu ra'suka' (may your head live) clearly referring to the whole person. The text also notes a difference of opinion regarding 'dam' (blood); the relied-upon view is that divorce occurs by adding to 'dam', but there is a narration that it does not. The word 'nafs' (self) definitively effects divorce.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', but in legal usage often means the whole person (slave or free)
عنق (unuq): Neck, used similarly to denote the whole person
فرج (farj): Private part; used in hadith to refer to women
نفس (nafs): Self, person

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a husband says to his wife 'Your neck is divorced'?
A) No divorce occurs because 'neck' is not the whole woman.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) The statement is invalid because it is not explicit.
D) Divorce occurs only if the husband intends the whole woman.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, divorce is effective when attributed to a part of the woman that is conventionally used to signify her whole person, based on Quranic and hadith evidence of synecdoche.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
5 min
Divorcing an Undivided Share of a Wife
Ruling on divorcing an undivided share like half or third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband divorces an undivided share of his wife and explain why it applies to the whole.

Core Idea
Divorcing an undivided share divorces the whole, as partial divorce is impossible.

This lesson presents a key Hanafi ruling on the effect of divorcing a fractional share of one's wife. The passage establishes that an undivided share is a valid subject for divorce, but because divorce cannot be partial, it necessarily applies to the entire woman. Understanding this rule clarifies a potential ambiguity in talaq and reinforces the principle that divorce is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Wording
Consider the husband's pronouncement: he says 'half of you is divorced' or 'one-third of you is divorced'. The wording explicitly refers to an undivided share (juz' shayi') of the wife.
The share must be an undivided fraction of the whole, not a specific part.
2
State the Ruling
According to the Hanafi school, this pronouncement results in a valid divorce (sahih) that applies to the entire wife, not just the stated fraction.
The ruling is unanimous within the Hanafi school and agreed upon by other schools.
3
Explain the Basis
An undivided share is a valid subject for legal transactions like sale, so by analogy (qiyas) it should also be valid for divorce. The text uses this reasoning: 'because the undivided share is a place for various transactions like sale and others'.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Note the Indivisibility
Divorce is inherently indivisible – a woman cannot be partially divorced. Therefore, when the divorce is applied to an undivided share, it necessarily encompasses the whole woman. The passage states: 'it is not divisible in respect of divorce, so it becomes established in the whole by necessity.'
This is a legal necessity (darurah) arising from the nature of divorce.
5
Apply to Practice
This ruling applies whether the wife is free or a slave, though the maximum number of divorces (three for a free woman, two for a slave) must be considered. The first such pronouncement counts as one divorce against the total allowable.
If a husband later tries to divorce the 'other half', it is redundant because the first already covered the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و کذلک ان طلق جزء شائعا مثل ان يقول نصفك او ثلثك طالق لان الجزء الشائع محل لسائر التصرفات كالبيع وغيره فكذايكون محلا للطلاق الا انه لا يتجزى فى حق الطلاق فيثبت في الكل ضرورة
And likewise, if he divorces an undivided share, like saying 'half of you' or 'one-third of you is divorced', because the undivided share is a place for various transactions like sale and others, so similarly it is a place for divorce, except that it is not divisible in respect of divorce, so it becomes established in the whole by necessity.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling: if a husband divorces an undivided share (juz' shayi') of his wife, such as saying 'half of you is divorced' or 'one-third of you is divorced', divorce occurs and it applies to the whole wife. The reason is that an undivided share, like half or third, is a valid subject for legal transactions such as sale. However, since divorce cannot be partial (a woman cannot be partially divorced), the effect encompasses the entire woman. This is a necessary consequence of the indivisibility of divorce in the case of an undivided share. This ruling applies to free women as well as slave-girls, with the appropriate differences in the maximum number of divorces.

Key Terms
جزء شائعا (juz' shayi'a): An undivided share or fraction (e.g., half, one-third) of the whole

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a husband says 'half of you is divorced'?
A) No divorce occurs
B) Half a divorce occurs
C) One full divorce occurs
D) The statement is treated as a metaphor and no legal effect

Key Takeaway
An undivided share of a woman is a valid subject for divorce, but because divorce is indivisible, it applies to the whole woman.
